<h1 id="mcetoc_1euu04s2027c">Chapter A7: FLIGHT PREPARATION<span style="font-size: 10pt; color: #ced4d9;">[VVE]</span></h1>
<article id="article_kJxaAXC8QUvynEj1rqMF" class="not_editable" data-id="kJxaAXC8QUvynEj1rqMF">
<h2 class="mceNonEditable" data-id="kJxaAXC8QUvynEj1rqMF" id="mcetoc_1in3piideho"><span class="shortcode">[A7.0]</span> <span class="name">Introduction Flight Preparation</span></h2>
<p><span data-preserver-spaces="true">UAS operations are, in principle, based on customer demands asides from test and training flights. The customer indicates requirements, whereby the [OPERATOR] ensures that the operation fits within the current legislation and regulations and technical frameworks. During flight preparation, the following matters should be considered:</span></p>
<ul>
<li><span data-preserver-spaces="true">Legal frameworks</span></li>
<li><span data-preserver-spaces="true">Operational frameworks</span></li>
<li><span data-preserver-spaces="true">Technical constraints</span></li>
<li><span data-preserver-spaces="true">Human constraints, see <a href="#article_Y0NJJgh3i5zasBwfbQH4">[A3.4] Physical aspects (IMSAFE)</a></span></li>
<li><span data-preserver-spaces="true">Risk analysis</span></li>
<li><span data-preserver-spaces="true">Mitigating measures</span></li>
</ul>
<p><span data-preserver-spaces="true">Good flight preparation is essential for conducting a safe flight operation. It is crucial that the remote pilot, together with his crew members, reviews all the steps before each flight.&nbsp;</span></p>
</article>
<p></p>
<article id="article_4fvWGKdg1PFBRY8suDm3" class="not_editable" data-id="4fvWGKdg1PFBRY8suDm3">
<h2 class="mceNonEditable" data-id="4fvWGKdg1PFBRY8suDm3" id="mcetoc_1itg4ja7123o"><span class="shortcode">[A7.1]</span> <span class="name">Information Resources</span></h2>
<p><span data-preserver-spaces="true">The pilot of [OPERATOR] utilises the following official information references when compiling the operational plan (OP):</span></p>
<ol style="list-style-type: lower-alpha;">
<li><span data-preserver-spaces="true">The ICAO (Aeronautical Chart The Netherlands ICAO 1: 500 000) map includes corrections on the map. See <a href="https://eaip.lvnl.nl/download/lvnl_icao_2023c.pdf">LVNL</a>. This map shows all areas (CTRs, EHP, EHR, EHD, ATZ etc) that are important for the execution of the flight. The card is published once a year by LVNL (at the beginning of flight season. i.e., March/April) and is available at LVNL or aircraft clubs. Any corrections which take place and affect the map during the year are shown in the AIP GEN 3. 2-8. </span></li>
<li>Aeronautical Information Products, AIP, at <a href="https://www.lvnl.nl/informatie-voor-luchtvarenden/publicaties-voor-luchtvarenden">https://www.lvnl.nl/informatie-voor-luchtvarenden/publicaties-voor-luchtvarenden</a></li>
<li>Amsterdam integrated briefing (for NOTAMs) at <a href="https://hbs.ixosystem.eu/ixo/login.php">LVNL Home Briefing</a></li>
<li>KNMI Aviation Weather at <a href="https://www.luchtvaartmeteo.nl">https://luchtvaartmeteo.nl</a></li>
<li>Special activities at <a href="https://en.lvnl.nl">https://en.lvnl.nl</a></li>
<li>Basic registration of addresses and buildings (BAG) viewer for current buildings: <a href="http://bagviewer.kadaster.nl">http://bagviewer.kadaster.nl</a></li>
<li>Information about Natura2000 area, railways, municipal boundaries, road network, waterways etc: <a href="https://www.pdok.nl/datasets">https://www.pdok.nl/datasets</a></li>
<li>High voltage cables: <a href="https://data.rivm.nl/apps/netkaart/">https://data.rivm.nl/apps/netkaart/</a></li>
<li>Antenna register: <a href="https://data.rivm.nl/apps/netkaart/">https://data.rivm.nl/apps/netkaart/</a></li>
</ol>
<p><span data-preserver-spaces="true">The quality and correctness of the above references have been checked and guaranteed.</span></p>
<p><span data-preserver-spaces="true">In addition, the following references can be used for flight preparation.</span></p>
<ul>
<li><span data-preserver-spaces="true">Internet resources: Google earth, Google Streetview (Please not that they might not be up-to-date)</span></li>
<li>Drone maps: <a href="https://map.godrone.nl">https://map.godrone.nl</a> and <a href="https://aeret.kaartviewer.nl/">https://aeret.kaartviewer.nl/</a></li>
<li>Natura2000 areas: <a href="https://www.natura2000.nl/gebieden">https://www.natura2000.nl/gebieden</a></li>
<li>KNMI Metar: <a href="https://www.knmi.nl/nederland-nu/luchtvaart/vliegveldwaarnemingen">https://www.knmi.nl/nederland-nu/luchtvaart/vliegveldwaarnemingen</a></li>
<li>KNMI TAF: <a href="https://www.knmi.nl/nederland-nu/luchtvaart/vliegveldverwachtingen">https://www.knmi.nl/nederland-nu/luchtvaart/vliegveldverwachtingen</a></li>
<li>Sun direction, time, date etc.: <a href="https://www.timeanddate.com">https://www.timeanddate.com</a></li>
<li>Weather check: <a href="https://www.windfinder.com">https://www.windfinder.com</a></li>
</ul>
<p><span style="font-size: 14pt;"><strong>Google Earth and Kadaster</strong></span></p>
<p style="margin: 0cm;"><span style="color: #0e101a;"><a href="https://earth.google.com/web/">Google Earth/Street view</a> allows you to view and interact with a wide variety of content, including but not limited to map and area data, business listings, traffic, reviews, and other related information provided by Google.&nbsp;</span><o:p></o:p><span data-preserver-spaces="true"><span style="color: #0e101a;">Advanced import functions of GIS (Geographic Information System) data can be used to prepare and plan a mission. Area, range and circumference on the ground are measurable, and you can print a high-resolution screenshot. Always check the location to see whether the data corresponds to the actual one.&nbsp;</span><o:p></o:p></span></p>
<p style="margin: 0cm;"><span data-preserver-spaces="true"><span style="color: #0e101a;"></span></span></p>
<p style="margin: 0cm;"><span data-preserver-spaces="true"><span style="color: #0e101a;">Bagviewer contains additional information about the owner of the area. See <a href="https://bagviewer.kadaster.nl/">https://bagviewer.kadaster.nl</a></span><o:p></o:p></span></p>
<p style="margin: 0cm;"><span data-preserver-spaces="true"><span style="color: #0e101a;">The BAG (Basisregistraties Addresses en Gebouwen) contains the official data of all addresses and buildings in the Netherlands. The municipalities are responsible for registering and updating this data.</span></span></p>
</article>
<p></p>
<article id="article_iRcEydy4mNduPJuTdAPY" class="not_editable" data-id="iRcEydy4mNduPJuTdAPY">
<h2 class="mceNonEditable" data-id="iRcEydy4mNduPJuTdAPY" id="mcetoc_1i1cgdiind3"><span class="shortcode">[A7.2]</span> <span class="name">Aeronautical Information Publication (AIP) Introduction</span></h2>
<p><span data-preserver-spaces="true">An AIP contains aeronautical information of a permanent character that is essential to airmen. The structure and content of the AIP are standardized through ICAO, the International Civil Aviation Organization. See <a href="https://eaip.lvnl.nl/web/2023-11-16-AIRAC/html/index-en-GB.html">https://eaip.lvnl.nl/web/2023-11-16-AIRAC/html/index-en-GB.html</a> for more information. Please note that the site is subject to change.</span></p>
<p><span data-preserver-spaces="true">AIPs normally have three parts - GEN (general), ENR (en route) and AD (aerodromes). The structure and contents of the document are standardised by international agreements through ICAO. AIPs are kept up-to-date by regular revision on a fixed cycle. For operationally significant changes in the information, the cycle known as the AIRAC (Aeronautical Information And Control) cycle is used: revisions (normally 1 per 28 days) are produced every 56 days (double AIRAC cycle) or every 28 days (single AIRAC cycle). These changes are received well in advance so that users of the aeronautical data can make necessary amendments.</span></p>
<p><span data-preserver-spaces="true">During the flight preparation, the crew members review the following sections:</span></p>
<ul>
<li><span data-preserver-spaces="true">ENR 1.4: ATS AIRSPACE CLASSIFICATION and ENR6-3.1 ENROUTE CHART ICAO for the airspace classification (see <a href="#article_TPSeWc9Tw9HtXYl0t7oo">[A7.3]</a>)</span></li>
<li><span data-preserver-spaces="true">GEN 2.7: SUNRISE / SUNSET for the uniform daylight period (see <a href="#article_nZXHw1eILunVzdPjn21Y">[A7.4]</a> )</span></li>
<li><span data-preserver-spaces="true">ENR 6-2.6: TRANSPONDER MANDATORY ZONES (see <a href="#article_lW2xffAu5EMBe5BZlmg4">[A7.5]</a>)</span></li>
<li><span data-preserver-spaces="true">ENR 5.1: PROHIBITED, RESTRICTED AND DANGER AREAS for non-flying zones or under specified conditions. (see <a href="#article_OPeSqML0Exq2VZpklG6m">[A7.6]</a>)</span></li>
<li><span data-preserver-spaces="true">ENR 5.2: MILITARY EXERCISE AND TRAINING AREAS for military low-flying areas (see <a href="#article_sko5Ym8pS3fsJ1pei1ad">[A7.10]</a>)</span></li>
<li><span data-preserver-spaces="true">ENR 5.3: OTHER ACTIVITIES OF DANGEROUS NATURE AND OTHER POTENTIAL HAZARD for gas venting sites, meteorological radiosonde balloon ascents sites, and model flying sites.&nbsp;</span></li>
<li><span data-preserver-spaces="true">ENR 5.5: AERIAL SPORTING AND RECREATIONAL ACTIVITIES for areas where paragliding, parachutists and unmanned balloons are allowed. (see also <a href="#article_yDKIi2RabqbKYp1n4suI">[A7.12]</a>)</span></li>
<li><span data-preserver-spaces="true">ENR 5.6: BIRD MIGRATION AND AREAS WITH SENSITIVE FAUNA for birds migrations, bird sanctuaries, and stork stations.</span></li>
<li><span data-preserver-spaces="true">ENR 6-5.2: LOW-FLYING ROUTES / AREAS CHART for low-flying routes in the Netherlands.</span></li>
<li><span data-preserver-spaces="true">ENR 6-5.3: BIRD SANCTUARIES, BIRD STRIKE RISK AND WETLAND AREA for nature reserves and bird stork stations.</span></li>
</ul>
<p><span data-preserver-spaces="true">The following sections describe these components and their features for a UAS flight operation, with any additional measures to be taken.</span></p>
</article>
<p></p>
<article id="article_TPSeWc9Tw9HtXYl0t7oo" class="not_editable" data-id="TPSeWc9Tw9HtXYl0t7oo">
<h2 class="mceNonEditable" data-id="TPSeWc9Tw9HtXYl0t7oo" id="mcetoc_1itg4ja7123p"><span class="shortcode">[A7.3]</span> <span class="name">Airspace Classification</span></h2>
<p><span data-preserver-spaces="true">In the AIP, at ENR 6 En route charts, ENR 6-3.1 is stated with the geographic division of the airspace including the lower limit and the upper limit of the different classes. For [OPERATOR] flying in the lowest air space is important (Class G) and in civil or military CTRs (Class C and D).</span></p>
<p><span data-preserver-spaces="true">The CTRs are recognizable areas around the airports. The uncontrolled airspace (Class G) is not indicated on both maps, but it is easy to define. You will be flying between the ground and 120 meters (400ft) AMSL or uncontrolled airspace or in a civil or military CTR. Other airspace classifications do not start until above 450 meters (1500ft) AMSL.&nbsp; </span></p>
<p><span data-preserver-spaces="true">See for example: <a href="https://vfrchart.lvnl.nl">https://vfrchart.lvnl.nl</a></span></p>
<p>Essential conditions/requirements associated with an airspace classification are:<br><span data-preserver-spaces="true"></span></p>
<ul>
<li><span data-preserver-spaces="true">The service you expect from the air traffic service.</span></li>
<li><span data-preserver-spaces="true">Which traffic is separated by the air traffic service.</span></li>
<li><span data-preserver-spaces="true">Minimum weather conditions under which (VFR) you are allowed to fly in this airspace.</span></li>
<li><span data-preserver-spaces="true">Two-way radio contact, a MODE S SSR transponder, and a flight plan or clearance are required to fly in this airspace.</span></li>
</ul>
<p><span data-preserver-spaces="true">The AIP, at ENR 1.4: ATS AIRSPACE CLASSIFICATION, states what the restrictions are for each airspace classification.</span><span style="text-decoration-line: underline; font-family: -apple-system, BlinkMacSystemFont, 'Segoe UI', Roboto, Oxygen, Ubuntu, Cantarell, 'Open Sans', 'Helvetica Neue', sans-serif;"></span></p>
</article>
<p></p>
<article id="article_nZXHw1eILunVzdPjn21Y" class="not_editable" data-id="nZXHw1eILunVzdPjn21Y">
<h2 class="mceNonEditable" data-id="nZXHw1eILunVzdPjn21Y"><span class="shortcode">[A7.4]</span> <span class="name">Uniform Daylight Period (UDP)</span></h2>
<p data-start="104" data-end="544">By default, UAS operations in the Netherlands are only allowed during daylight hours, as defined by the Uniform Daylight Period (UDP). These times vary throughout the year. The table in GEN 2.7 of the AIP (<a data-start="312" data-end="383" class="" href="https://www.lvnl.nl/informatie-voor-luchtvarenden/publicaties-voor-luchtvarenden">link</a>) lists the UDP in UTC (Coordinated Universal Time — abbreviation in French order). To convert to Dutch local time, add 1 hour in winter and 2 hours in summer.</p>
<p data-start="546" data-end="613">Note: Operators with an exemption may also fly outside the UDP.</p>
</article>
<p></p>
<article id="article_lW2xffAu5EMBe5BZlmg4" class="not_editable" data-id="lW2xffAu5EMBe5BZlmg4">
<h2 class="mceNonEditable" data-id="lW2xffAu5EMBe5BZlmg4" id="mcetoc_1itg4ja7123r"><span class="shortcode">[A7.5]</span> <span class="name">Transponder Mandatory Zone (TMZ)</span></h2>
<p><span data-preserver-spaces="true">A Transponder Mandatory Zone (TMZ) is an airspace of defined dimensions in which transponder equipment is mandatory.&nbsp;</span></p>
<p><span data-preserver-spaces="true">In the Netherlands, a transponder obligation applies in the entire airspace, except in Class G below 365 meters (1200 ft) AMSL. All the flight operations designated as a TMZ shall carry and operate Secondary Surveillance Radar (SSR) transponders capable of operating on Mode S (Mode S SSR-transponder).</span></p>
<p><span data-preserver-spaces="true">Most TMZs start at an altitude where no UAS flights are allowed. A TMZ that may be important for UAS flights is the TMZ North Sea Area Amsterdam. UAS flights above the North Sea are only permitted if a working transponder and two-way radio contact can be maintained with the air traffic service.</span></p>
<p><span data-preserver-spaces="true">A TMZ is active from Monday to Friday during office hours (09:00 - 17:00) with a lower limit of 1200ft AMSL. The lower limit will be raised outside these times and on Saturdays, Sundays, and public holidays. It will also differ per TMZ. Therefore, for the above-mentioned days, those who are concerned about the lower limit are required to check it during flight preparation.</span></p>
<p></p>
<p><span data-preserver-spaces="true"><strong>Use of Radio</strong>&nbsp;</span></p>
<p><span data-preserver-spaces="true">The obligation for two-way radio contact can be determined by:</span></p>
<ul>
<li><span data-preserver-spaces="true">The airspace classification,</span></li>
<li><span data-preserver-spaces="true">Setting up a radio mandatory zone (RMZ)</span></li>
</ul>
<p><span data-preserver-spaces="true">In the CTRs, the obligation for two-way radio contact is established by airspace classification C or D. To comply with the obligation for two-way radio contact, you must have the correct equipment (antenna of at least 10 meters height, radio set) and personnel who have the ability of radiotelephony. RMZ and TMZ are listed in the AIP ENR 2.2.</span><span style="text-decoration: underline;"></span></p>
</article>
<p></p>
<article id="article_OPeSqML0Exq2VZpklG6m" class="not_editable" data-id="OPeSqML0Exq2VZpklG6m">
<h2 class="mceNonEditable" data-id="OPeSqML0Exq2VZpklG6m" id="mcetoc_1itg4ja7123s"><span class="shortcode">[A7.6]</span> <span class="name">Chart Prohibited, Restricted and Danger (PDR) Areas and Temporary Reserved Airspace (TRA)</span></h2>
<p><span data-preserver-spaces="true">In section ENR 5.1 of the AIP, it is stated that the areas in the Netherlands that are not or only partially accessible. Those areas where long-term restrictions are applied are recognised with the following abbreviations:</span></p>
<ul>
<li><span data-preserver-spaces="true">EHP (Prohibited) with a number</span></li>
<li><span data-preserver-spaces="true">EHR (Restricted) with a number</span></li>
<li><span data-preserver-spaces="true">EHD (Danger) with a number</span></li>
<li><span data-preserver-spaces="true">ATZ (Aerodrome Traffic Zone) with a name</span></li>
</ul>
<p><span data-preserver-spaces="true">(EH is the nationality prefix of the Netherlands)</span></p>
<p><span data-preserver-spaces="true">If you encounter such an area on the map, you can find this in the AIP in section ENR 5.1. The restrictions are stated in the right column (<a href="https://www.lvnl.nl/informatie-voor-luchtvarenden/publicaties-voor-luchtvarenden">www.ais-netherlands.nl</a>). You use these restrictions when preparing for flight in such an area and include them in the briefing, for example, when applying for or checking a NOTAM or when contacting the area manager and/or requesting an exemption.</span></p>
<p><span data-preserver-spaces="true">Also consult the NOTAMs for temporary restricted, prohibited or dangerous areas.</span><span data-preserver-spaces="true"></span></p>
</article>
<p></p>
<article id="article_XY7SWCuLaAZfLo45oFTR" class="not_editable" data-id="XY7SWCuLaAZfLo45oFTR">
<h2 id="mcetoc_1fl6mjmj22d16" class="mceNonEditable" data-id="XY7SWCuLaAZfLo45oFTR"><span class="shortcode">[A7.7]</span> <span class="name">Operational and Technical Limitations</span></h2>
<p><span data-preserver-spaces="true">Based on the customer request, the pilot, together with the payload operator, investigates the possibilities and which payload can be set to process the customer request.</span></p>
<p><span data-preserver-spaces="true">Check the technical and operational limitations in Chapter B1: Technical Description UAS.</span></p>
</article>
<p></p>
<article id="article_41xHw3CDpzrLzISeLUB4" class="not_editable" data-id="41xHw3CDpzrLzISeLUB4">
<h2 class="mceNonEditable" data-id="41xHw3CDpzrLzISeLUB4" id="mcetoc_1in3piidehr"><span class="shortcode">[A7.8]</span> <span class="name">Natura 2000</span></h2>
<p>Natura 2000 (hereafter N2000) is the umbrella term for areas protected under the Birds and Habitats Directives. According to these European directives, member states must protect specific species and their natural habitats to preserve biodiversity. In the Netherlands, this applies to over 160 areas. As of January 1, 2024, the Environmental Law (<a href="https://iplo.nl/regelgeving/omgevingswet/">Omgevingswet</a>) has come into effect. This is a Dutch umbrella law that includes the relevant rules.</p>
<p>The Dutch Zoning Regulation does not include Natura 2000 areas. The management of these areas in the Netherlands is under the responsibility of Provinces and Rijkswaterstaat. In most cases, Provinces (sometimes via Environmental Services) are authorized to grant exemptions for drone take-offs and landings in N2000 areas. However, for two areas, national legislation still prohibits drone flights: the Wadden Sea and the Oosterschelde.</p>
<p>Extensive (inter)national research shows that the impact of drones on nature is very limited. To avoid disturbing birds and animals, it is recommended to fly higher than:</p>
<p>&gt; 50 m for quadcopters &nbsp;<br>&gt; 70 m for fixed-wing drones</p>
<p>Flight Preparation</p>
<p>To obtain permission for a drone flight in an N2000 area, follow these steps:</p>
<p>1. Information via a website on N2000&nbsp;<br>For the most up-to-date information, consult the <a href="https://www.bij12.nl">BIJ 12 website</a> (the knowledge center of the provinces). It contains all the management plans for N2000 areas.</p>
<p>2. Information via a flight guidance app (Aeret)<br>Through the app, you can select a specific area and get a link to the management plan for that area on the BIJ 12 website. The target species map is from the EU database (which includes the key species). The app also provides information on who to contact to request permission.</p>
<p>3. Follow the flowchart/menu from Bureau Waardenburg (<a href="https://droneconsultancy.nl/wp-content/uploads/2024/04/Professioneel-Dronegebruik-stroomschema-N2000-gebieden.pdf">Drone Consultancy website</a>)<br>Check the impact of your drone flight. If the impact is too great, try adjusting your flight to minimize the impact. Send this flowchart and the considerations to the area manager.</p>
<p>4. Menu of regulations<br>Contact the person responsible for the management plan to follow the requirements for specific operators from the menu of regulations and indicate that the flights will be logged in the flight guidance app and can be reviewed by an enforcement officer.</p>
<p>Note: The Ministry of Infrastructure and Water Management (I en W) will actively distribute the menu of regulations to provinces and nature management organizations so that it will eventually be included in management plans.</p>
<p>Specific regulations apply to the Wadden Sea and Oosterschelde due to additional national legislation.</p>
<p>Oosterschelde<br>In practice, it is often the case that the area manager (game warden) can approve drone flights for public benefit, provided they are present during the flight. Legal text for Oosterschelde: <a href="https://wetten.overheid.nl/BWBR0038733/">https://wetten.overheid.nl/BWBR0038733/</a></p>
<p>Wadden Sea<br>Legal text for Wadden Sea: <a href="https://wetten.overheid.nl/BWBR0010215/">https://wetten.overheid.nl/BWBR0010215/</a> &nbsp;<br>For the Wadden Sea, an exemption can be requested based on Articles 3 or 19 of the Air Traffic Act 2014 via (ILT) email: aviation-approvals@ILenT.nl for the following activities: &nbsp;<br>a. Emergency and rescue operations &nbsp;<br>b. Supervision and investigation by authorized bodies &nbsp;<br>c. Monitoring the condition of high-voltage power lines and pipelines &nbsp;<br>d. Measurements in the interest of public health, management, or science &nbsp;<br>e. Occasional photo and film recordings for news reporting or informational purposes</p>
</article>
<p></p>
<article id="article_5uXqi5TJAhyK2nlBQPck" class="not_editable" data-id="5uXqi5TJAhyK2nlBQPck">
<h2 class="mceNonEditable" data-id="5uXqi5TJAhyK2nlBQPck" id="mcetoc_1itg4ja7123t"><span class="shortcode">[A7.9]</span> <span class="name">Bird Nesting Area</span></h2>
<p>Bird nesting areas are sometimes partially and/or completely closed off for activities at certain times of the year. Flying in these areas is risky for both the UAS and the birds. If flying in these areas is necessary, an exemption must be applied for (lead time 6 weeks).&nbsp;</p>
<p>Contact information:<br>Human Environment and Transport Inspectorate (ILT) / Aviation<br>tel: +31 (0) 70 456 2260<br>email: <a href="mailto:aviation-approvals@ILT.nl">aviation-approvals@ILT.nl</a></p>
</article>
<p></p>
<article id="article_sko5Ym8pS3fsJ1pei1ad" class="not_editable" data-id="sko5Ym8pS3fsJ1pei1ad">
<h2 class="mceNonEditable" data-id="sko5Ym8pS3fsJ1pei1ad"><span class="shortcode">[A7.10]</span> <span class="name">Military and Civil Low-flying Areas</span></h2>
<p style="margin: 0cm;"><span style="color: #0e101a;">The low flying areas and routes were established in the 1950s. Their utilisation is regulated by aviation law. Both sports and military aircraft make use of the low-flying regions. In the latter case, you could think of military exercises or training flights at low altitudes (below 300 meters/1000 ft). A military low-flight route is 3,700 meters wide, and a minimum height of 75 meters (250 ft) shall be maintained. Helicopters can even fly 30 meters (100 ft) or lower in some areas. Military low-flying areas can be identified by the code GLV.</span><o:p></o:p></p>
<p style="margin: 0cm;"><span data-preserver-spaces="true"><span style="color: #0e101a;">Section ENR 5.2 of the AIP contains information about low-flying areas: <a href="https://www.lvnl.nl/informatie-voor-luchtvarenden/publicaties-voor-luchtvarenden">eAIP</a></span><o:p></o:p></span></p>
<p style="margin: 0cm;"><span style="color: #0e101a;"><o:p>&nbsp;</o:p></span><span data-preserver-spaces="true"><span style="color: #0e101a;"></span></span></p>
<p style="margin: 0cm;"><span data-preserver-spaces="true"><span style="color: #0e101a;">If the UA flight is performed above 30 metres, permission is required for this operation. A&nbsp;NOTAM must be requested and published no later than 24 hours before the flight in which the operation with the UAS is announced. During these flights, it is mandatory to fly together with an observer.</span></span></p>
<p style="margin: 0cm;"><span data-preserver-spaces="true"><span style="color: #0e101a;"></span></span></p>
<p style="margin: 0cm;"><span data-preserver-spaces="true"><span style="color: #0e101a;">An indicative chart of these areas can also be found on <a href="https://map.godrone.nl">Dronemap</a>&nbsp;at Godrone and <a href="https://aeret.kaartviewer.nl">AERET</a>.</span><o:p></o:p></span><span data-preserver-spaces="true"><span style="color: #0e101a;"></span></span></p>
</article>
<p></p>
<article id="article_h829OYLxiCSIimzWhegm" class="not_editable" data-id="h829OYLxiCSIimzWhegm">
<h2 id="mcetoc_1fmhmla0m5v" class="mceNonEditable" data-id="h829OYLxiCSIimzWhegm"><span class="shortcode">[A7.11]</span> <span class="name">Simulated forced landing areas</span></h2>
<p style="margin: 0cm;"><span style="color: #0e101a;">The AIP on map ENR 6-5.2 also lists areas where the manned aircraft are allowed to fly low to practice an emergency landing. This must be taken into account during flight preparations. Flying in this area is carried out with at least 1 observer and this is included in the risk analysis (flight preparation) per flight and the briefing.</span></p>
<p style="margin: 0cm;"><span style="color: #0e101a;">&nbsp;</span></p>
<p style="margin: 0cm;">Permission from the air traffic controller is also required for these areas. Furthermore, a NOTAM must be published no later than 24 hours before the flight. In the NOTAM, the flight with the UAS is announced if it concerns flights that take place above an altitude of 40 meters within this airspace in which low flying is allowed.</p>
</article>
<p></p>
<article id="article_yDKIi2RabqbKYp1n4suI" class="not_editable" data-id="yDKIi2RabqbKYp1n4suI">
<h2 class="mceNonEditable" data-id="yDKIi2RabqbKYp1n4suI" id="mcetoc_1itg4ja7123v"><span class="shortcode">[A7.12]</span> <span class="name">Sports and Recreational Flying Activities</span></h2>
<p style="margin: 0cm;"><span style="color: #0e101a;">Flights near uncontrolled airports are allowed. The airport operator is contacted for this. Use a limit of 3 km. This also applies to glider airfields, hang glider fields, heliports, and parachuting locations. This information can be found at <a href="https://vfrchart.lvnl.nl">LVNL (eAIP)</a>, ENR 5.5 AERIAL SPORTING AND RECREATIONAL ACTIVITIES.</span></p>
</article>
<p></p>
<article id="article_uzamKM8VAFXfz0hnMWJj" class="not_editable" data-id="uzamKM8VAFXfz0hnMWJj">
<h2 id="mcetoc_1gf5tglhd6t" class="mceNonEditable" data-id="uzamKM8VAFXfz0hnMWJj"><span class="shortcode">[A7.13]</span> <span class="name">Flying Near an Uncontrolled Airport</span></h2>
<p style="margin: 0cm;"><span data-preserver-spaces="true"><span style="color: #0e101a;">Dutch Aviation Act (Wet Luchtvaart), Article 5.3 says:</span></span></p>
<p style="margin: 0cm;"><span data-preserver-spaces="true"><span style="color: #0e101a;">It is prohibited to participate in air traffic or to provide air traffic control services in such a way that persons or property are or may be endangered as a result.</span></span></p>
<p style="margin: 0cm;">&nbsp;</p>
<p style="margin: 0cm;"><span data-preserver-spaces="true"><span style="color: #0e101a;">This article applies to all air traffic, including UAS operators. To fly in the vicinity of uncontrolled airports, you should coordinate with the owner/operator of the airport concerned. The distance to which coordination is required from the uncontrolled airport remains arbitrary.</span></span></p>
<p style="margin: 0cm;">&nbsp;</p>
<p style="margin: 0cm;"><span data-preserver-spaces="true"><span style="color: #0e101a;">Chapter 7: Flight Preparation provides more information about flying in the vicinity of uncontrolled airports (such as military and civilian CTRs).</span></span></p>
<p style="margin: 0cm;">&nbsp;</p>
<p><span style="text-decoration: underline;">Further Reading Section</span></p>
<ul>
<li><a href="#article_1etefZRZYdUqIyntvRQS">[A7.14] Flying in or near an Aerodrome Traffic Zone (ATZ)</a></li>
</ul>
</article>
<p></p>
<article id="article_1etefZRZYdUqIyntvRQS" class="not_editable" data-id="1etefZRZYdUqIyntvRQS">
<h2 id="mcetoc_1fmhmla0m60" class="mceNonEditable" data-id="1etefZRZYdUqIyntvRQS"><span class="shortcode">[A7.14]</span> <span class="name">Flying in or near an Aerodrome Traffic Zone (ATZ)</span></h2>
<p style="margin: 0cm;"><span style="color: #0e101a;">ATZ is an air traffic area designated around an airport that has been established to protect airport traffic. For civil ATZs, the following text is therefore included in the AIP:</span></p>
<p style="margin: 0cm;"><span data-preserver-spaces="true"><span style="color: #0e101a;">'An ATZ has been established around the following aerodromes. The activities conducted at these aerodromes make it undesirable for other aircraft, not engaged in these activities, to penetrate or otherwise disturb the traffic pattern. In this case, the ATZ is primarily reserved for use by aircraft participating in the activities of the aerodrome. Overflying aircraft are strongly recommended to stay clear of the ATZ.'</span></span></p>
<p style="margin: 0cm;">&nbsp;</p>
<p style="margin: 0cm;"><span data-preserver-spaces="true"><em><span style="color: #0e101a;">For military ATZ this text is included in the AIP:</span></em></span></p>
<p style="margin: 0cm;"><span data-preserver-spaces="true"><span style="color: #0e101a;">'Outside the published operating hours of controlled military aerodromes, general aviation (glider and motor flying) activities may take place in the CTR under a special exemption. For the protection of these activities, ATZs (a circular area around the aerodrome with a radius of 2 NM from GND to 1500 ft AAL) has been established within the relevant military CTRs. Aircraft, crossing a military CTR outside the normal operating hours of the aerodrome with the permission of the relevant Flight Information Centre, shall keep clear of the ATZ. '</span></span></p>
<p style="margin: 0cm;"><span style="color: #0e101a;">&nbsp;</span></p>
<p style="margin: 0cm;"><span data-preserver-spaces="true"><span style="color: #0e101a;">For professional flying with a UAS in or in the vicinity of an ATZ, the same applies to flying in the vicinity of uncontrolled airports. See chapter VVE13; coordination with the operator of the airport, or the manager of the airspace within which the ATZ is located, is necessary. Preferably, the flight could be scheduled outside the activities of the ATZ.</span></span></p>
<p style="margin: 0cm;"><span style="color: #0e101a;">&nbsp;</span></p>
<p style="margin: 0cm;"><span data-preserver-spaces="true"><span style="color: #0e101a;">Please note that the military CTR becomes an RMZ outside opening hours (see ENR 2.2.4).</span></span></p>
</article>
<p></p>
<article id="article_Htor7PKVt4Ke4we0mlHW" class="not_editable" data-id="Htor7PKVt4Ke4we0mlHW">
<h2 class="mceNonEditable" data-id="Htor7PKVt4Ke4we0mlHW" id="mcetoc_1i1cgdiindb"><span class="shortcode">[A7.15]</span> <span class="name">Special Airspace Activities / Closures</span></h2>
<p>In addition to the fixed airspace restrictions and closures included in the AIP, airspace measures are also taken that occur occasionally. These measures are made available by the Minister of Defense or by the Minister of Infrastructure and the Environment and made known to airmen by NOTAM. This concerns, for example, the establishment of temporary areas with restrictions to protect air traffic between themselves (for example, when dismantling bombs, large events, shooting model missiles, etc.).&nbsp;<br>This concerns, for example, the establishment of temporary areas with restrictions to protect air traffic between themselves (for example, when dismantling bombs, large events, shooting model missiles, etc.)</p>
<p>As an extra service, LVNL places the drawings associated with the temporarily set restricted or closed areas on the site of the operational helpdesk at <a href="https://en.lvnl.nl/services/temporary-airspace-restrictions">LVNL temporary airspace restrictions</a> (tijdelijke luchtruimsluitingen, Dutch only).</p>
</article>
<p></p>
<article id="article_UhIbfSPd2g1wLxlr3afd" class="not_editable" data-id="UhIbfSPd2g1wLxlr3afd">
<h2 id="mcetoc_1fl6mjmj22d1f" class="mceNonEditable" data-id="UhIbfSPd2g1wLxlr3afd"><span class="shortcode">[A7.16]</span> <span class="name">Controlling Mass and Balance</span></h2>
<p style="margin: 0cm;"><span style="color: #0e101a;">To guarantee a stable flight, the mass and balance of the UAS must be always following the supplier's specifications. The UAS performance is affected not only by the weight of the payload but also by the position of that weight on the UAS. Determining the centre of gravity (CoG) is necessary for this. The CoG is the theoretical point where the entire weight of the UAS resides. If the UAS is attached from a cable at exactly that point, it would hang perfectly balanced. Usually, the manufacturer supplies software to determine this.&nbsp;</span><span style="color: #0e101a;">The CoG is described (if known) in the technical section of the OM, i.e., Chapter B1: Technical Description UAS.</span></p>
<pre id="tw-target-text" class="tw-data-text tw-text-large tw-ta" dir="ltr" data-placeholder="Vertaling"></pre>
</article>
<p></p>
<article id="article_shMM5J3RbSjGiNnbD8wz" class="not_editable" data-id="shMM5J3RbSjGiNnbD8wz">
<h2 id="mcetoc_1fl6mjmj22d1g" class="mceNonEditable" data-id="shMM5J3RbSjGiNnbD8wz"><span class="shortcode">[A7.17]</span> <span class="name">The Maximum Acceptable Total Mass</span></h2>
<p>In aviation, the quantity of 'mass' is used and not 'weight'. Nevertheless, the quantity of weight is widely used in the technical documentation on UAS.</p>
<ul>
<li>MTOM = Maximum Take-off Mass</li>
<li>BEM = Basic Empty Mass</li>
</ul>
<p>There are many more terms in use that have to do with the mass in aviation, however, these two are sufficient for the UAS. UAS are aircraft that almost always transport something, such as a camera. The bare device is called a platform and the camera - or whatever you want to transport - is called payload. With few exceptions, the payload is replaceable/interchangeable, certainly with the more expensive UAS for professional use. If the payload is not interchangeable, only 1 mass (including battery/accumulator) is specified by the manufacturer, which is also the MTOM. If the payload is interchangeable, the manufacturer specifies the mass of the platform including battery/accumulator (BEM). Additionally, an MTOM or the maximum mass of the payload are specified.</p>
<p>MTOM = BEM + maximum mass of the payload</p>
<p>The manufacturer determines this MTOM based on, among other things, design, structure/material, balance, thrust and ultimately testing.</p>
<p>The values ​​of the mass must always remain within the specifications of the supplier of the UAS.</p>
</article>
<p></p>
<article id="article_8qLQ9tVRDfI12U2TZzyf" class="not_editable" data-id="8qLQ9tVRDfI12U2TZzyf">
<h2 class="mceNonEditable" data-id="8qLQ9tVRDfI12U2TZzyf" id="mcetoc_1itg4ja71240"><span class="shortcode">[A7.18]</span> <span class="name">Reading and Submitting Notice to Airmen (NOTAM)</span></h2>
<p>NOTAM messages contain information that is also essential for a safe flight operation but cannot (yet) be included in the AIP. NOTAM messages are:</p>
<ol>
<li>No permanent nature (less than three months)</li>
<li>Important in the short term (AIP is only updated periodically)</li>
</ol>
<p>Civil CTR</p>
<p>A NOTAM must be issued for the following drone flights:</p>
<ul>
<li>drone flight outside UDP (N.B. Flying outside UDP is not permitted without an exemption);</li>
<li>drone flight in a civil low flying area;</li>
<li>drone flight in or under controlled airspace, which goes higher than 120 m and/or has a flying area with a radius greater than 500 m.</li>
</ul>
<p>To do so, you can send an e-mail <a href="mailto:ops_helpdesk@lvnl.nl">(ops_helpdesk@lvnl.nl</a>) to the Operational Helpdesk at LVNL with the following details:</p>
<ul>
<li>place name location;</li>
<li>coordinate flight area in degrees, minutes and seconds;</li>
<li>radius flight area;</li>
<li>altitude flight;</li>
<li>date and time(s), indicate whether local time or UTC;</li>
<li>contact details.</li>
</ul>
<p>Military CTR</p>
<p>A NOTAM must be issued by the FDNO for the following drone flights:&nbsp;</p>
<ul>
<li>drone flight in a military CTR for which you have received permission from local air traffic control;</li>
<li>drone flight in or under controlled military airspace, which goes higher than 40 m and/or has a flying area with a radius greater than 500 m.</li>
</ul>
<p>You will find contact details of the FDNO (in part 1 GEN) local military air traffic control (in part 3 AD) in the military AIP: <a href="https://english.defensie.nl/topics/milaip-military-aeronautical-information-publication">https://english.defensie.nl/topics/milaip-military-aeronautical-information-publication</a></p>
<p>For more information, please refer this site: <a href="https://www.lvnl.nl/diensten/drones#anchor-7c3ea530246d4558a61bb30a0b82e3ff">https://www.lvnl.nl/diensten/drones#anchor-7c3ea530246d4558a61bb30a0b82e3ff</a>.</p>
<p></p>
<p>Below, the example of NOTAM is shown.</p>
<p>NOTAM for EHLW</p>
<p>V0086 / 17 -</p>
<p>[US DOD PROCEDURAL NOTAM] STANDARD INSTRUMENT DEPARTURE (SID) AMENDMENT LEE 3 DEP MSA LWD TAC CHANGED, MIL AIP EHLW FROM AD 2-11 TO 2-30. READ 1700FT INSTEAD OF 1500FT. 05 APR 15:48 2017 UNTIL 21 JUN 23:59 CREATED: 05 APR 15:48 2017</p>
<p><span style="font-size: 10pt;">Station: EHLW is the 4-letter code for the entire Dutch airspace (Leeuwarden airport)</span><br><span style="font-size: 10pt;">Notam id: the reference code.</span></p>
<p><span style="font-size: 10pt;">This is a "date/time group". The time is always UTC.</span><br><span style="font-size: 10pt;">In the summer the Dutch local time is UTC +2 and in the winter UTC +1.</span></p>
</article>
<p></p>
<h1 id="mcetoc_1evd1jh2m5ap"></h1>
<article id="article_7mJlHIMmQ8H88jcIuMZn" class="not_editable" data-id="7mJlHIMmQ8H88jcIuMZn">
<h2 class="mceNonEditable" data-id="7mJlHIMmQ8H88jcIuMZn" id="mcetoc_1in3piideht"><span class="shortcode">[A7.19]</span> <span class="name">Weather METAR / TAF GA weather chart (Weather observations) </span></h2>
<p>A METAR (Meteorological Aerodrome Report) is a meteorological message consisting of a snapshot of the local weather at an airport or a weather station. This message is compiled every half hour (: 25 and: 55). A METAR always has the same construction.</p>
<p>The significant difference between a METAR and TAF (Terminal Aerodrome Forecast) is that the METAR is a weather report and the TAF is a weather forecast. A METAR is therefore always 'old news'.&nbsp;</p>
<p>Both the METAR and TAF start with a date-time group. The TAF also indicates for which time it is valid. Note, as with NOTAMs, the UTC is used (Dutch winter time is UTC +1 and Dutch summer time is UTC +2. So convert to Dutch local time).&nbsp;</p>
<p>Below you can see the two examples of the aerodrome weather observation data, META and TAF.</p>
<p><strong>METAR Schiphol</strong></p>
<p>METAR EHAM 280925Z 23010KT 9999 VCSH FEW015 FEW022TCU 18/14 Q1016 NOSIG=</p>
<table border="1" style="border-collapse: collapse; width: 99.7478%; height: 372px;">
<tbody>
<tr style="height: 21px;">
<td style="width: 13.2136%; height: 21px;">EHAM4</td>
<td style="width: 83.1756%; height: 21px;">letter code for Schiphol</td>
</tr>
<tr style="height: 43px;">
<td style="width: 13.2136%; height: 43px;">280925Z</td>
<td style="width: 83.1756%; height: 43px;">the 28th of the month at 9:25 am "Z". Zulu time is UTC. In the Netherlands, we have 'A' or 'Alpha' time</td>
</tr>
<tr style="height: 21px;">
<td style="width: 13.2136%; height: 21px;">23010KT</td>
<td style="width: 83.1756%; height: 21px;">&nbsp;230: wind direction. 10kt: wind speed in knots</td>
</tr>
<tr style="height: 65px;">
<td style="width: 13.2136%; height: 65px;">9999</td>
<td style="width: 83.1756%; height: 65px;">
<p><span>Reported in a four figure group (e.g. 0400 = 400 metres; 8000 = 8 km) up to but excluding 10 km; 9999 = 10km or more; 0000 = less than 50 metres visibility</span></p>
</td>
</tr>
<tr style="height: 138px;">
<td style="width: 13.2136%; height: 138px;">VCSH</td>
<td style="width: 83.1756%; height: 138px;">Special codes for it are displayed in this block. Here it says VCSH. SH stands for showers. VC stands for vicinity, or 'in the environment. There are more codes like RA (rain), FG (fog), BR (haze). There may also be a '-' or '+' in front of the weather. It only concerns precipitation or thunderstorms. The minus sign means 'light' and the plus sign means heavy. ++TSSHRA means 'very heavy rain with thunderstorms (TS)'.&nbsp;</td>
</tr>
<tr style="height: 21px;">
<td style="width: 13.2136%; height: 21px;">FEW015</td>
<td style="width: 83.1756%; height: 21px;">
<p>The cloud cover is always in 'eighths'. FEW means 'a little' or 1/8 to 3/8 sunshade cover</p>
</td>
</tr>
<tr style="height: 21px;">
<td style="width: 13.2136%; height: 21px;">FEW022TCU</td>
<td style="width: 83.1756%; height: 21px;">
<p>The addition TCU indicates the type of cloud. CU means Cumulus. The 'T' indicates 'Towering', or 'Constructive'</p>
</td>
</tr>
<tr style="height: 21px;">
<td style="width: 13.2136%; height: 21px;">18/14</td>
<td style="width: 83.1756%; height: 21px;">
<p>Temperature: 18 Celsius. Dew point (DP) temperature: 14 Celsius. If these two temperatures are close together, the air is humid. They can also be equal. Then it is normally rainy weather. The DP can never be more than the temperature</p>
</td>
</tr>
<tr style="height: 21px;">
<td style="width: 13.2136%; height: 21px;">Q1016</td>
<td style="width: 83.1756%; height: 21px;">
<p>The QNH is the atmospheric pressure corrected to mean sea level. The pressure is 1016 hPa</p>
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td style="width: 13.2136%;">NOSIG</td>
<td style="width: 83.1756%;">
<p>'No Significant changes' or no changes expected in the next two hours after the observation time</p>
</td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table>
<p></p>
<p><strong>TAF Schiphol</strong></p>
<p>The TAF uses the same codes as the METAR. It is, however, a forecast. Below the typical TAF, codes are explained with the above TAF as an example.</p>
<p>TAF EHAM 280438Z 2806/2912 21008KT 9999 FEW020 SCT040 BECMG 2807/2810 25015KT BECMG 2817/2820 19005KT CAVOK PROB30 2902/2906 6000 SCT005=</p>
<table border="1" style="border-collapse: collapse; width: 99.7478%; height: 168px;">
<tbody>
<tr style="height: 21px;">
<td style="width: 11.1857%; height: 21px;">2806/2912</td>
<td style="width: 85.2035%; height: 21px;">
<p>Validity period: from the 28th at 06:00 UTC to the 29th at 12:00 UTC. For this example, the period is 30 hours. This can be longer and shorter</p>
</td>
</tr>
<tr style="height: 21px;">
<td style="width: 11.1857%; height: 21px;">BECMG</td>
<td style="width: 85.2035%; height: 21px;">
<p>'Becoming', in other words, the change to come. You also often see the code 'FM', from ... with a specific time or time&nbsp;block (2617/2820). It is therefore very important here that&nbsp;</p>
<p>you look at the correct date/time group and also convert to the Dutch time</p>
</td>
</tr>
<tr style="height: 21px;">
<td style="width: 11.1857%; height: 21px;">PROB30</td>
<td style="width: 85.2035%; height: 21px;">
<p>PROB30, PROB40 or indicate the probability that certain&nbsp;weather will come. PROM stands for 'probability'</p>
</td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table>
<p>The pilot checks the weather forecast on the intended date and no later than 24 hours in advance at KNMI: For <a href="https://www.knmi.nl/nederland-nu/luchtvaart/vliegveldwaarnemingen">METAR</a>, for <a href="https://www.knmi.nl/nederland-nu/luchtvaart/vliegveldverwachtingen">TAF</a>.</p>
<p><a href="https://www.luchtvaartmeteo.nl">The KNMI Aviation weather site</a>&nbsp;is an alternative.&nbsp;</p>
<p>The local wind force and wind direction are measured with a handheld wind speed meter.&nbsp;</p>
<p>Operating UAS is strictly prohibited in the following weather conditions.</p>
<p><strong>No flying conditions [OPERATOR]*</strong></p>
<p>N.B. * These parameters depend on the type of drone. You should therefore check the specific usage restrictions for the drone(s) to be used in the user manual as soon as a drone is deployed to fly safely outside these restrictions.</p>
<ul>
<li>Ice formation in the air (hail, snow, sleet);</li>
<li>Wind force 5 Bft (10 m/s) or higher;&nbsp;</li>
<li>Temperature is lower than minus 10 Celsius or higher than 40 Celsius;</li>
<li>Cloud coverage (fog, haze);</li>
<li>When the rules of a specific type of airspace cannot be complied with (distance from clouds);</li>
<li>In rain or drizzle;</li>
<li>Near CB cloud cover (thunderstorm);</li>
<li>High risk of turbulence.</li>
</ul>
</article>
<p></p>
<article id="article_PXL41rUnSFPOw56YFSJH" class="not_editable" data-id="PXL41rUnSFPOw56YFSJH">
<h2 class="mceNonEditable" data-id="PXL41rUnSFPOw56YFSJH" id="mcetoc_1i1cgdiinde"><span class="shortcode">[A7.20]</span> <span class="name">Operations in Open Category</span></h2>
<p>This Operations Manual describes all procedures and regulations relating to operations in the Specific Category. However, depending on the situation, it may also be the case that flights in the Open Category are carried out.</p>
<p>When can a flight be operated under the Open Category?</p>
<ul>
<li>The UA weighs (at take-off) a maximum of 25 kg;</li>
<li>Maximum altitude of 120 metres;</li>
<li>Not carrying hazardous substances;</li>
<li>Not letting anything fall out of the drone;</li>
<li>Visual line of sight (VLOS).</li>
</ul>
<p>Source: <a href="https://www.rijksoverheid.nl/onderwerpen/drone/vraag-en-antwoord/regels-drone-laag-risico">Dutch Government Site</a></p>
<p>In addition, the following preconditions apply that depend on the type of drone:</p>
<p><img style="display: block; margin-left: auto; margin-right: auto;" src="https://firebasestorage.googleapis.com/v0/b/drone-consultancy-test.appspot.com/o/images_new%2F82HBR8nAdGoyZMspNn99%2Fopen%20category.jpg?alt=media&amp;token=783dddea-502c-4651-bec3-55ef8616fe96" alt="" width="638"></p>
<p style="text-align: center;">Figure: Source EASA 2022</p>
<p style="text-align: left;"></p>
<p style="text-align: left;">Please note that in addition to the EU regulations, there are additional national regulations that should be included. Please refer to the sources in chapter <a href="#article_QZuY26xOr1hkhFaKs0ue">[D1.1.1] Regulations in EU and the Netherlands</a>.</p>
</article>
<p></p>
<article class="not_editable" data-id="9mUGopu7c6QxVwgCgSR1" id="article_9mUGopu7c6QxVwgCgSR1">
<h2 class="mceNonEditable" data-id="9mUGopu7c6QxVwgCgSR1" id="mcetoc_1itg4ja71241"><span class="shortcode">[A7.21P]</span> <span class="name">Operational Plan (Briefing)</span></h2>
<p>The operational plan is intended as a briefing indicating what the UAS operation is, where and when it will take place, what the limitations are - whether or not imposed by law and/or regulation - and what the risks are and mitigation measures.&nbsp;</p>
<p>The operational plan is brought to the flight location. It is the result of flight preparation, risk analysis and looking into the checklists.</p>
<p>The operational plan at [OPERATOR] consists of three parts:</p>
<ol>
<li>1.Quick scan: the initial test of the feasibility of the assignment. [OPERATOR] assesses the feasibility of the assignment for the ground and air risk components of the Operational Plan. See <a href="#article_HZqMod6IwVtQbkSvAi0H">[APPENDIX C1] Flight Plan and Check Lists</a>.</li>
<li>
<p>Drafting the Operational Plan that includes the following consideration. Also drawing them to the map(s):</p>
<ul>
<li>The Operational Volume with the risk buffer.</li>
<li>Take-off and Landing Area</li>
<li>Alternative take-off and landing location</li>
<li>Location of the flight crew in the operational area on the map, see also&nbsp;<a href="#article_HZqMod6IwVtQbkSvAi0H">[APPENDIX C1] Flight Plan and Check Lists</a>.</li>
<li>Risk analysis in advance</li>
</ul>
</li>
<li>Risk analysis and checklists on site (day of execution). The latest operational checklists, logbook pages (UAS &amp; Batteries) and an overview of emergency procedures, see [Appendix C.2] Flight preparation 3: day of operation.</li>
</ol>
<p>Phase 1: Quick scan</p>
<p>The quick scan consists of a short, quick check whether the flight is feasible within the preconditions of the operating permit and the ConOps.</p>
<ul>
<li>The required UAS has a maximum wingspan of 3 meters or less;</li>
<li>The MTOM of the UAS is 25 kilograms or less;</li>
<li>VLOS operation;</li>
<li>The operational volume, which may be located in inhabited areas, is only feasible with controlled ground area;</li>
<li>The operational volume, which may also be in lightly populated areas (sparsely populated), is only feasible with controlled ground area;</li>
<li>The flight is conducted in controlled or uncontrolled airspace, where there is little chance of encountering manned aircraft;</li>
<li>Flights are carried out in the relevant airspace on the basis of the privileges obtained;</li>
<li>Flights are performed at a maximum altitude of 120 metres AGL.</li>
</ul>
<p>Phase 2: Drafting Operational Plan</p>
<p>If the quick scan shows that the operation is feasible, the Operational Plan is filled in further. An example can be found in&nbsp;<a href="#article_HZqMod6IwVtQbkSvAi0H">APPENDIX C1] Flight Plan and Check Lists</a>. The format consists of a number of parts:</p>
<ol>
<li>General flight data with purpose of operation, address, details, time, crew members, etc;</li>
<li>Flight characteristics such as VLOS, number and duration of flights;</li>
<li>Meteorological conditions (forecast);</li>
<li>The Operational Volume with the risk buffers where a number of maps are placed that make it clear where the Operational Volume is located, what risks are in the vicinity and what risk buffers are used:
<ol>
<li>Map from Google Maps or Google Earth with the take-off and landing area, the alternate take-off and landing locations, the location of the flight crew, the Ground Risk Buffer and a map of the airspace e.g. via Godrone. See also <a href="#article_HZqMod6IwVtQbkSvAi0H">APPENDIX C1] Flight Plan and Check Lists</a>.</li>
<li>For determining the size of the Ground Risk Buffer, see section A6.4 Operational Volume;</li>
</ol>
</li>
<li>Airspace classification:
<p class="p2">nagaan per luchtruimrisico waar we ons bevinden en wat de risico's en maatregelen zijn die worden genomen;</p>
</li>
<li>Checklist ground risks: per risk which measures will be taken;</li>
<li>Risk analysis for the site (probability-impact matrix);</li>
<li>Emergency Response Plan;</li>
<li>Approval of Accountable Manager and date.</li>
</ol>
<p>Phase 3: Risk analysis and use of checklists</p>
<p>On the day itself, a risk analysis is also carried out, the health of crew members is questioned and a number of checklists are used. These checklists are also included in Appendix C.</p>
<p>There are a number of ways to use checklists:</p>
<p>Method checklist</p>
<table border="1" style="border-collapse: collapse; width: 100.039%;">
<tbody>
<tr>
<td style="width: 25.7131%;">Name</td>
<td style="width: 74.198%;">Description</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td valign="top" class="td1" style="width: 25.7131%;">
<p class="p1">Challenge &amp; Respond</p>
</td>
<td style="width: 74.198%;">The observer reads the item to be checked. The UAS Pilot checks the item to be checked for the correct status. The UAS Pilot responds with the status. The observer puts a checkmark on the checklist.</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td valign="top" class="td1" style="width: 25.7131%;">
<p class="p1">Read &amp; Do</p>
</td>
<td style="width: 74.198%;">A crew member reads out the item to be checked. Another crew member performs the action after which the crew member who read out the item checks it off on the checklist.</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td style="width: 25.7131%;">Flow</td>
<td style="width: 74.198%;">A standard pattern of actions performed according to the checklist. This checklist can be performed solo.</td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table>
<p></p>

<p>It is the responsibility of the PIC to ensure that the flight or series of flights is/are planned and executed in compliance with the applicable regulations and safety and meets all the requirements of [OPERATOR].</p>
