<article id="article_hHSumH80HQwUl3jwculQ" class="not_editable" data-id="hHSumH80HQwUl3jwculQ">
<h2 class="mceNonEditable" data-id="hHSumH80HQwUl3jwculQ" id="mcetoc_1in3piidfj5"><span class="shortcode">[C6.4]</span> <span class="name">ERP procedure and emergency contact list</span></h2>
<p>[OPERATOR] creates a checklist/call list for the Operational Plan that is implemented for each flight in the Specific category. The list includes emergency contact numbers and establishes the agreed-upon roles for the operation.</p>
<p>Depending on the operation, it is determined who will be the responsible person (ERM = Emergency Response Manager) for handling the emergency situation. Please refer to the Operational Plan filled out by [OPERATOR] at the time of application for details.</p>
</article>
<p class="p1">See appendix C1 for an example of operational Plan of [OPERATOR].pdf', see appendix C6 for ERP checklist.</p>
<p></p>
<h1 id="mcetoc_1fmhnu757akr"><div class="page-break"></div>Chapter C7. SAFETY EQUIPMENT<span style="font-size: 10pt; color: #ced4d9;">[BVUE]<br></span></h1>
<article id="article_HyKh9Afktlil7QjVQUZW" class="not_editable" data-id="HyKh9Afktlil7QjVQUZW">
<h2 class="mceNonEditable" data-id="HyKh9Afktlil7QjVQUZW" id="mcetoc_1in3piidfj6"><span class="shortcode">[C7.1]</span> <span class="name">General Safety Equipment</span></h2>
<p><em>Reference of this chapter: AMC3 UAS.SPEC.030(3)(e) Application for an operational authorisation</em></p>
<p><span>The ERP, for medium/high level of assurance, should indicate the means to be used by the UAS operator to respond to an emergency, which may include one or more of the following:</span></p>
<ul>
<li><span>facilities, infrastructure, and equipment;</span></li>
<li><span>extinguishing means, e.g. fire extinguishers;</span></li>
<li><span>personal protective equipment, e.g. protective clothing, high-visibility clothing, helmets, goggles, gloves;</span></li>
<li><span>medical means, including first-aid kits;</span></li>
<li><span>communication means, e.g. phones (landline and mobile), walkie-talkies, aviation radios, internet; and others.</span></li>
</ul>
<p><span>The person(s) in charge of the emergency response means should have an updated record of the available means that are indicated in point, including their number and status (e.g. expiry date of perishable means).</span></p>
<p><span>In this chapter, the safety equipment in use by [OPERATOR] is described.</span></p>
</article>
<p></p>
<article id="article_Fw81E6wKY4dLOVUs4jB5" class="not_editable" data-id="Fw81E6wKY4dLOVUs4jB5">
<h2 class="mceNonEditable" data-id="Fw81E6wKY4dLOVUs4jB5"><span class="shortcode">[C7.2]</span> <span class="name">CO2 Fire Extinguisher</span></h2>
<p>[OPERATOR] owns a <em>CO<sub>2</sub> fire extinguisher (this is not suitable for extinguishing a LiPo fire).</em><em></em></p>
<p>The extinguisher has been approved by the Dutch Ministry of the Interior (Ministerie van Binnenlandse Zaken), Fire Service Department, and must comply with the European Standard EN3. It must also bear the CE mark CE0879.</p>
<p>The fire extinguisher must also be inspected periodically (once every two years) by a recognized organization/institute. When purchasing the extinguisher, the supplier will issue the production date, which must be visibly written on the label.</p>
<p>The fire extinguisher must be at the take-off and landing site and ready for immediate use.</p>
<p><strong></strong></p>
<p><strong>What to do with a LiPo fire</strong></p>
<p>With a UAS, the most fire-hazardous part is the LiPo battery. To understand how to act in the event of a fire caused by an ignition of a LiPo battery, it is good to know what causes such a fire. A LiPo can ignite thanks to; heat from the outside, overvoltage, under-voltage, short circuit, damage or perforation from outside and/or swelling. These are all possible causes or indicators that indicated a (future) LiPo fire. Upon detecting any of the above defects immediately;</p>
<ul>
<li>Determine the location of the nearest fire extinguisher.</li>
<li>Informing colleagues present and informing the responsible person present.</li>
<li>In the event of a fire with a risk to the environment, immediately notify colleagues and authorities such as the fire brigade. The nearest emergency services are listed in the flight plan.&nbsp;</li>
<li>Leave the battery burning – 20 minutes is a safe margin – and if necessary use a fire extinguisher at the fire location for the environment, keep a safe distance and DO NOT inhale the smoke.</li>
</ul>
</article>
<p></p>
<article id="article_APv2L8mi1EY0iQvg0PTL" class="not_editable" data-id="APv2L8mi1EY0iQvg0PTL">
<h2 class="mceNonEditable" data-id="APv2L8mi1EY0iQvg0PTL"><span class="shortcode">[C7.3]</span> <span class="name">First Aid Kit</span></h2>
<p>A first aid kit is always available at the office of [OPERATOR] and also during the operation on location to provide support in case of accidents. The first aid box is the type BHV (bedrijfshulpverlener, emergency response officer) and complies with all guidelines. The crew and/or employees present should be aware of the location of the first aid box.</p>
</article>
<p></p>
<article id="article_Qn9c52E1mxqxvRM4KbRc" class="not_editable" data-id="Qn9c52E1mxqxvRM4KbRc">
<h2 class="mceNonEditable" data-id="Qn9c52E1mxqxvRM4KbRc" id="mcetoc_1in3piidfj9"><span class="shortcode">[C7.4]</span> <span class="name">Safety Vests</span></h2>
<p>To increase the visibility of the flight crew, it is mandatory to wear recognizable safety vests during the operation. Wearing the vests ensures mutual recognition, but also distinguishes them from uninvolved persons and any authorities present. The crew wear a safety vest. These vests are also mandatory on construction sites, along with other personal protective equipment. Site regulations will be met as prescribed.</p>
</article>
<p></p>
<article id="article_zlypMVF40yVaBP3rYdhb" class="not_editable" data-id="zlypMVF40yVaBP3rYdhb">
<h2 class="mceNonEditable" data-id="zlypMVF40yVaBP3rYdhb" id="mcetoc_1in3piidfja"><span class="shortcode">[C7.5]</span> <span class="name">Other Safety Equipment</span></h2>
<p>During the operation, the flight crew is aware of the applicable safety regulations at the flight location. At construction and industrial locations, the flight crew wears head protection and reinforced footwear. All local safety regulations must be followed unless they conflict with the safe and successful performance of the operation itself.</p>
</article>
<h1 id="mcetoc_1f6k76vm36vh"></h1>
<h1 id="mcetoc_1f6k76vm36vi"></h1>
<h1 id="mcetoc_1f6k76vm36vj"></h1>
<h1 id="mcetoc_1euiupec21g4"><div class="page-break"></div>PART D: TRAINING AND INSTRUCTIONS</h1>
<p></p>
<h1 id="mcetoc_1fmhnu757al0"><div class="page-break"></div>Chapter D1: GENERAL INSTRUCTIONS FOR FLIGHT OPERATIONS <span style="font-size: 10pt; color: #ced4d9;">[VUE]<br></span></h1>
<article id="article_QZuY26xOr1hkhFaKs0ue" class="not_editable" data-id="QZuY26xOr1hkhFaKs0ue">
<h2 class="mceNonEditable" data-id="QZuY26xOr1hkhFaKs0ue" id="mcetoc_1i1cgdiinei"><span class="shortcode">[D1.1]</span> <span class="name">Regulations in EU and the Netherlands</span></h2>
<p class="p1">This Operations Manual is in accordance with the following Dutch Aviation Act (and the other Aviation Act) and European regulations:</p>
<ul>
<li>Besluit bewijzen van bevoegdheid voor de luchtvaart</li>
<li><a href="https://wetten.overheid.nl/BWBR0023145/">Besluit luchtvaartuigen 2008</a></li>
<li>Besluit vluchtuitvoering</li>
<li>Besluit luchtverkeer 2014</li>
<li>Besluit burgerluchthavens</li>
<li>(Besluit) RTL (Regeling toezicht luchtvaart)</li>
<li>Regeling inschrijving burgerluchtvaartuigen</li>
<li>Regeling erkenningen luchtwaardigheid 2008</li>
<li>Regeling veilig gebruik luchthavens en andere terreinen</li>
<li>Regeling vluchtuitvoering</li>
<li>Regeling luchtverkeersdienstverlening</li>
<li><a href="https://wetten.overheid.nl/BWBR0044598/">Regeling Onbemande Luchtvaartuigen (ROL)</a></li>
<li>Wetboek van Strafrecht</li>
<li><a href="https://www.easa.europa.eu/regulations/sera-standardised-european-rules-air">SERA (Standard European Rules of the Air)</a></li>
<li><a href="https://www.easa.europa.eu/document-library/easy-access-rules/easy-access-rules-unmanned-aircraft-systems-regulation-eu">Uitvoeringsverordening (EU) 2019/945 and 947 for unmanned systems</a></li>
</ul>
<p class="p1">For UAS flights are especially important:<br>1. EU legislation, concerning EU 2019/947 and 2019/945<br>2. National legislation (different per EU member state, see A7.24 flying in other EU countries)</p>
<p class="p1"><span style="text-decoration: underline;"><strong>UAS EU Regulations</strong></span></p>
<p class="p1"><span style="font-size: 12pt;">The UAS Regulations consists of two separate, but interlinked regulations as follows:</span></p>
<ul>
<li class="p1"><span style="font-size: 12pt;">“Commission Implementing Regulation (EU) 2019/947 on the procedures and rules for the operation of unmanned aircraft”.<span class="s1" style="font-family: -apple-system, BlinkMacSystemFont, 'Segoe UI', Roboto, Oxygen, Ubuntu, Cantarell, 'Open Sans', 'Helvetica Neue', sans-serif;">&nbsp;</span></span></li>
<li>
<p>“Commission Delegated Regulation (EU) 2019/945 on unmanned aircraft and on third country operators of unmanned aircraft systems”.</p>
</li>
</ul>
<p><span style="font-size: 12pt;">In this chapter, the summary of the regulation regarding the specific category is described. The complete </span>regulations are published at <a href="https://eur-lex.europa.eu/legal-content/EN/TXT/PDF/?uri=CELEX:32019R0947&amp;from=EN">EUR-Lex for the implementing regulation</a> and <a href="https://eur-lex.europa.eu/legal-content/EN/TXT/PDF/?uri=CELEX:32019R0945&amp;from=EN">EUR-Lex for the delegated regulation.</a></p>
<p><strong><span id="Subject_Matter" class="mw-headline">Subject Matter</span></strong></p>
<p>This Regulation lays down detailed provisions for the operation of unmanned aircraft systems as well as for personnel, including remote pilots and organisations involved in those operations.</p>
<p><strong>Applicability of the Regulation</strong></p>
<ul>
<li class="p1">The regulations are applicable throughout the EU and the Netherlands from 1 July 2019.</li>
<li class="p1">The term 'operation of unmanned aircraft systems' does not include indoor UAS operations.</li>
</ul>
<p><strong>Summary</strong></p>
<p>UAS Operations shall be performed in the 'open', 'specific' or 'certified' category defined respectively in <a href="https://eur-lex.europa.eu/legal-content/EN/TXT/HTML/?uri=CELEX:32019R0947&amp;from=EN#d1e698-45-1">Articles 4</a>, <a href="https://eur-lex.europa.eu/legal-content/EN/TXT/HTML/?uri=CELEX:32019R0947&amp;from=EN#d1e762-45-1">5</a>, and <a href="https://eur-lex.europa.eu/legal-content/EN/TXT/HTML/?uri=CELEX:32019R0947&amp;from=EN#d1e828-45-1">6</a>, subject to the following conditions:</p>
<ol style="list-style-type: lower-alpha;">
<li>UAS operations in the 'open' category shall not be subject to any prior operational authorisation, nor to an operational declaration by the UAS operator before the operation takes place;</li>
<li>UAS operations in the 'specific' category shall require an operational authorisation issued by the competent authority pursuant to <a href="https://eur-lex.europa.eu/legal-content/EN/TXT/HTML/?uri=CELEX:32019R0947&amp;from=EN#d1e1416-45-1">Article 12</a> or an authorisation received in accordance with <a href="https://eur-lex.europa.eu/legal-content/EN/TXT/HTML/?uri=CELEX:32019R0947&amp;from=EN#d1e1823-45-1">Article 16,</a> or, under circumstances defined in <a href="https://eur-lex.europa.eu/legal-content/EN/TXT/HTML/?uri=CELEX:32019R0947&amp;from=EN#d1e762-45-1">Article 5(5)</a>, a declaration to be made by a UAS operator.</li>
<li>UAS operations in the 'certified' category shall require the certification of the UAS pursuant to <a href="https://eur-lex.europa.eu/legal-content/EN/TXT/PDF/?uri=CELEX:32019R0945&amp;from=EN">Delegated Regulation (EU) 2019/945</a> and the certification of the operator and, where applicable, the licensing of the remote pilot.</li>
</ol>
<p>UAS operations in the 'open' category must comply with the operational limitations set out in <a href="https://eur-lex.europa.eu/legal-content/EN/TXT/HTML/?uri=CELEX:32019R0947&amp;from=EN#d1e32-60-1">Part A of the Annex to the implementing Regulation</a>.</p>
<p>If the requirements for the' open' category cannot be met, the UAS operator must obtain an operational authorisation to operate in the 'specific' category from the competent authority in the Member State where it is registered. When applying for this, the operator must perform a risk assessment and if the competent authority considers that the operational risks are adequately mitigated it will issue an operational authorisation which may apply to a single operation or to a number of operations.</p>
<p>UAS operations in the 'specific' category must comply with the operational limitations set out in their authorisation.</p>
<p>See also: <a href="https://www.easa.europa.eu/document-library/easy-access-rules/easy-access-rules-unmanned-aircraft-systems-regulation-eu">Easy Access Rules for Unmanned Aircraft Systems (Regulation (EU) 2019/947 and Regulation (EU) 2019/945)</a></p>
<p><strong>UAS Regulations in the Netherlands</strong></p>
<p>In the Netherlands, in addition to EU regulations, the following Aviation Act should be taken in account: Aviation Act: <a href="https://wetten.overheid.nl/BWBR0005555/">https://wetten.overheid.nl/BWBR0005555/</a>. And more specifically the Air Traffic Decree: <a href="https://wetten.overheid.nl/BWBR0035899/">https://wetten.overheid.nl/BWBR0035899/</a>. This decree regulates matters that are important for UAS flights, such as Article 10 about dropping payloads.&nbsp;</p>
<p>For flying with drones, it is important;</p>
<p><a href="https://wetten.overheid.nl/BWBR0044598/2021-04-22">Regulations on Unmanned Aircraft (Regeling Onbemande Luchtvaarttuigen, ROL)</a></p>
<p>Regarts to Commission Implementing Regulation (EU) nr. 2019/947 van de Commissie of 24 May 2019 on rules and procedures for the operation of unmanned aircraft (PbEU 2019, L 152), the <a href="https://wetten.overheid.nl/jci1.3:c:BWBR0005555&amp;artikel=1.5&amp;g=2022-09-06&amp;z=2022-09-06">articles 1.5</a>, <a href="https://wetten.overheid.nl/jci1.3:c:BWBR0005555&amp;artikel=1.6&amp;g=2022-09-06&amp;z=2022-09-06">1.6</a>, <a href="https://wetten.overheid.nl/jci1.3:c:BWBR0005555&amp;artikel=1.7&amp;g=2022-09-06&amp;z=2022-09-06">1.7</a> and <a href="https://wetten.overheid.nl/jci1.3:c:BWBR0005555&amp;artikel=5.10&amp;g=2022-09-06&amp;z=2022-09-06">5.10, first paragraph, section a, of the Aviation Act</a>, the <a href="https://wetten.overheid.nl/jci1.3:c:BWBR0035899&amp;artikel=4&amp;g=2022-09-06&amp;z=2022-09-06">articles 4</a>, <a href="https://wetten.overheid.nl/jci1.3:c:BWBR0035899&amp;artikel=4a&amp;g=2022-09-06&amp;z=2022-09-06">4a</a>, <a href="https://wetten.overheid.nl/jci1.3:c:BWBR0035899&amp;artikel=19&amp;g=2022-09-06&amp;z=2022-09-06">19, third paragraph</a>, and <a href="https://wetten.overheid.nl/jci1.3:c:BWBR0035899&amp;artikel=20&amp;g=2022-09-06&amp;z=2022-09-06">20 of the Air Traffic Decision 2014</a> and <a href="https://wetten.overheid.nl/jci1.3:c:BWBR0010629&amp;artikel=13&amp;g=2022-09-06&amp;z=2022-09-06">Section 13, subsections 4 and 5, of the Aviation Licences Decree</a>;</p>
<p><a href="https://wetten.overheid.nl/BWBR0044597/2021-04-22">Zoning Regulations for Unmanned Aircraft:</a></p>
<p>The GoDrone application made available by the government and LVNL for UAS flying in the Netherlands can be used for this purpose.<span style="background-color: #f1c40f;"><span class="s1"></span></span></p>
</article>
<p></p>
<article id="article_JYKECKHcj57ZN89ru39T" class="not_editable" data-id="JYKECKHcj57ZN89ru39T">
<h2 class="mceNonEditable" data-id="JYKECKHcj57ZN89ru39T" id="mcetoc_1i1cgdiinej"><span class="shortcode">[D1.2]</span> <span class="name">Classification of Airspace</span></h2>
<p>The entire airspace in the Netherlands is divided into different airspaces. Each airspace has its qualification (class). Each airspace class has different rules and restrictions with which pilots shall bind. The rights and obligations associated with the 7 airspace classifications (Class A to G) have been established by the International Civil Aviation Organisation (ICAO) and are therefore used worldwide. In Europe, the Standardised European Rules of the Air (SERA) airspace classification system is applied. The SERA airspace classification is described in <a href="https://eaip.lvnl.nl/web/2023-04-06-AIRAC/html/eAIP/EH-ENR-1.4-en-GB.html">ENR 1.4, ATS Airspace Classification and Description.</a>&nbsp;You will also find a map on&nbsp;<a href="https://vfrchart.lvnl.nl/">the VFR chart The Netherlands</a>.&nbsp;</p>
<div class="page" title="Page 29">
<div class="section">
<div class="layoutArea">
<div class="column">
<p>In the AIP, at&nbsp;<a href="https://eaip.lvnl.nl/web/2023-04-06-AIRAC/html/eAIP/EH-ENR-6-en-GB.html">ENR 6, En Route Charts</a>, you will find map ENR 6.3.1. which shows the geographical layout of the airspace including the lower limit and the upper limit of the different airspace classes. Certainly, only the lower airspace is applied for flying a UAS. Class G airspace is the rest of the area where no class has been assigned. In section ENR 1.4 of the AIP, you will find criteria for being permitted to conduct any flight operations at each class. Class G is particularly important for flying a UAS. If you are permitted to fly civilian or military CTRs, class C and D will be also of interest.</p>
<p>Any other aspects are also described in&nbsp;<a href="https://nl.ivao.aero/training/luchtruim">IVAO Netherlands, section Luchtruim</a> (Dutch language only).</p>
</div>
</div>
</div>
</div>
</article>
<p></p>
<article id="article_Kte1lesJaUhNRVu4fUVo" class="not_editable" data-id="Kte1lesJaUhNRVu4fUVo">
<h2 id="mcetoc_1fmhnu757al3" class="mceNonEditable" data-id="Kte1lesJaUhNRVu4fUVo"><span class="shortcode">[D1.3]</span> <span class="name">VFR regulations</span></h2>
<p>Visual Flight Rules (VFR) are a set of regulations under which a pilot conducts a flight operation in weather conditions generally clear enough to allow the pilot to see where the UAS is flying. It is therefore that the operation is intended to be conducted in visual meteorological conditions (VMC). Under VFR it should be circumvented when the weather conditions cause low visibility such as thick clouds or heavy precipitation.</p>
</article>
<p></p>
<article id="article_G0gTiCyPozF0JkmgUFsR" class="not_editable" data-id="G0gTiCyPozF0JkmgUFsR">
<h2 class="mceNonEditable" data-id="G0gTiCyPozF0JkmgUFsR" id="mcetoc_1i1cgdiinek"><span class="shortcode">[D1.4]</span> <span class="name">General Meteorology</span></h2>
<p>The influence of weather and climate is very important to complete UAS operations safely. Especially a small object such as UAS is more affected by the weather conditions. It is, therefore, more crucial to be aware of the weather forecast surrounding the operation all the time.</p>
<p>The weather information around the UAS operation area shall be obtained from the Dutch national weather service, The Royal Netherlands Meteorological Institute (KNMI). On their&nbsp;<a href="https://www.knmi.nl/over-het-knmi/about">website</a>&nbsp;you can obtain the following information:</p>
<ul>
<li>Maximum temperature</li>
<li>Minimum temperature</li>
<li>Probability of precipitation</li>
<li>Precipitation quantities</li>
<li>Percentage of sunshine</li>
<li>Probability of potential mist</li>
<li>Wind speeds (Beaufort wind force scale)</li>
<li>Wind directions</li>
</ul>
<p>Additionally, a METAR weather report can be checked at this&nbsp;<a href="https://www.knmi.nl/nederland-nu/luchtvaart/vliegveldwaarnemingen">site</a>. METAR is a format for reporting weather information. A typical METAR contains data for the temperature, dew point, wind direction and speed, precipitation, cloud cover and heights, visibility and barometric pressure. A METAR may also contain information on precipitation amounts, lighting and other information which would be of great interest to UAS pilots. The other meteorological information webpages are;</p>
<ul>
<li><a href="https://www.buienradar.nl/">Buienradar</a>&nbsp;(radar information)</li>
<li><a href="https://www.knmi.nl/nederland-nu/luchtvaart/vliegveldverwachtingen">Terminal Aerodrome Forecast</a>&nbsp;(TAF)</li>
</ul>
<p>The PIC is responsible for checking the weather 24 hours before every scheduled flight operation. The weather conditions must be within the UAS limitations. Prior to the taking-off, both the weather condition and also the wind condition should be checked. The wind condition can be found at&nbsp;<a href="https://www.windfinder.com/?utm_source=www.windfinder.com&amp;utm_medium=web&amp;utm_campaign=redirect#3/49.5042/9.5421">windfinder</a>&nbsp;(www.windfinder.com) or&nbsp;<a href="https://www.windguru.cz/53">windguru</a>&nbsp;(www.windguru.com).</p>
<p>During the entire operation, the weather should be visually and continuously monitored. If a mobile phone with an internet connection is available, the current weather radar information could be obtained. The operator could also use a windmeter or a thermometer to measure the weather conditions.</p>
</article>
<p></p>
<article id="article_DhQm3p4B7L3EXaO0qYKL" class="not_editable" data-id="DhQm3p4B7L3EXaO0qYKL">
<h2 class="mceNonEditable" data-id="DhQm3p4B7L3EXaO0qYKL"><span class="shortcode">[D1.5]</span> <span class="name">Icing Conditions</span></h2>
<p>Icing occurs due to a combination of cold temperatures and humidity in the air. The accreted ice layer causes many dangerous problems during the UAS flight such as reduced lift and increased drag forces, significantly decreased angle of attack, strong vibrations and structural imbalances of UAS, malfunction of control surfaces and air pressure sensors, reduced visibility and improper radio communication.</p>
<p>The bigger the water droplets, the more likely they will hit the drone. Small water droplets follow the airflow (downwash) more. As a result, they hit only the bottom of the side of the UAS. The faster the UAS flies, the more droplets hit the UAS.</p>
<p>The three main types of ice accretion, in order of their hazard to flying, are as follows:</p>
<ul>
<li>Clear ice: Often clear and smooth. Supercooled water droplets, or freezing rain, strike a surface but do not freeze instantly. Relatively large drops of water.</li>
<li>Rime ice: Rough and opaque, formed by supercooled drops rapidly freezing on impact.</li>
<li>Hoar frost: White ice crystals deposited on the ground or loosely attached to exposed objects. It forms from water vapour in an above freezing atmosphere coming in contact with a solid surface whose temperature is below zero Celsius.</li>
</ul>
<p><strong>Instruction for crew members</strong></p>
<p>When the above-mentioned icing occurs, no flight operation shall be executed and is shall be rescheduled. Since different UAS types have different icing risks, the crew should check the manufacturer's manual for specific limits and warnings if unsure.</p>
<p>When the operation is conducted under cold weather condition, the UAS should be always flown as low as possible. Also, the UAS should be flown in short proximity to the pilot. Besides, the speed of the UAS should be kept lower. And every 5 minutes of the operation the UAS should be examined if any icing occurs to the UAS. Monitor the behaviour of the UAS in the air and immediately land the UAS and dry it, if there is any suspicion that icing occurs.</p>
</article>
<p></p>
<article id="article_u6S19kJ8tnIBziluhJoS" class="not_editable" data-id="u6S19kJ8tnIBziluhJoS">
<h2 id="mcetoc_1fmhmla0m70" class="mceNonEditable" data-id="u6S19kJ8tnIBziluhJoS"><span class="shortcode">[D1.6]</span> <span class="name">Turbulence</span></h2>
<p>Turbulence is defined as the ‘irregular motion’ of the air from eddies and vertical airflows. It is different from wind that flows horizontally from different directions, a force that is easily measured by weather services. A turbulent flow can occur when the wind changes directions or a storm arrives at a locality.&nbsp;</p>
<p>A UAS operation could be disturbed by turbulence when the UAS is flown behind windmills or in the vicinity of buildings, or any other (man-made) obstructions.</p>
<p>There are mainly three mechanisms which turbulence occurs:</p>
<ul>
<li>Thermal Turbulence is caused by solar heating of the surface, which in turn heats the lower atmosphere resulting in unequal convective currents.</li>
<li>Mechanical Turbulence results from wind flowing over or around irregular terrain or man-made obstructions.</li>
<li>Orographic turbulence arises from the friction of the air when blowing against mountainous elevations.&nbsp;</li>
</ul>
<p><strong>The Venturi-effect</strong></p>
<p>The Venturi effect is a case where fluid flows through a narrow tube. The velocity of the fluid increases as it flows through the narrower tube while the pressure decreases, due to the first law of thermodynamics.&nbsp;</p>
<p>When standing between high-rise buildings, you might feel that the wind blowing around you is faster and stronger. This accelerating wind, i.e., turbulence, is the result of squeezed air through narrow spaces when several high-rise buildings stand close to one another (See the schematic figure below).</p>
<p><img style="display: block; margin-left: auto; margin-right: auto;" src="https://firebasestorage.googleapis.com/v0/b/drone-consultancy-test.appspot.com/o/images_new%2F82HBR8nAdGoyZMspNn99%2FWind%20in%20stedelijk%20gebied%20venturi.jpg?alt=media&amp;token=cb897f06-910b-4ac5-a525-93f690252c24" alt=""></p>
<p style="text-align: center;"><img src="https://firebasestorage.googleapis.com/v0/b/drone-consultancy-test.appspot.com/o/images_new%2F82HBR8nAdGoyZMspNn99%2FWind%20om%20gebouwen.jpg?alt=media&amp;token=ada2c92e-79ba-4d85-a1a3-fa469c0f80eb" alt="">&nbsp;</p>
<p><strong>Instruction for crew members</strong></p>
<p>In specific UAS operations, such as in the vicinity of industry and/or urban areas, turbulence as a result of the venturi effect can be expected. Also, there is a strong possibility of a lost link in these areas as a result of EM disturbance. During the flight preparation, the Venturi-effect should be taken into account by properly mapping out the obstacles present in the vicinity and estimating the effect.</p>
<p>The UAS pilot should operate the UAS in manual mode and fly it slowly. The pilot should have been trained for this circumstance during his training.</p>
</article>
<p></p>
<article id="article_MhhbfO15ovijJByvwykA" class="not_editable" data-id="MhhbfO15ovijJByvwykA">
<h2 class="mceNonEditable" data-id="MhhbfO15ovijJByvwykA" id="mcetoc_1in3piidfjb"><span class="shortcode">[D1.7]</span> <span class="name">Wind Shear and Wind Gusts</span></h2>
<p>Wind shear is a change in wind speed and/or direction over a short distance. It can occur either horizontally or vertically and is most often associated with strong temperature inversions or density gradients. There are many causes of wind shear. In UAS flight operation, the common low-altitude wind shear includes frontal wind shear, topography-induced wind shear and micro downburst. To avoid wind shear conditions as much as possible, you could sometimes anticipate it from observing the type of clouds (see the below figure).</p>
<p>&nbsp;<img style="display: block; margin-left: auto; margin-right: auto;" src="https://firebasestorage.googleapis.com/v0/b/drone-consultancy-test.appspot.com/o/images_new%2F82HBR8nAdGoyZMspNn99%2FSoorten%20wolken.jpg?alt=media&amp;token=9a64426c-c2c4-4ffe-ae2b-cd7ddf7294b8" alt=""></p>
<p style="text-align: center;"><em>Figure: Classification of clouds</em></p>

<p>Table: Cloud identification</p>
<p>The orange cloud types (5 and 6) shown are very unstable and hazardous variants, so it is not advisable to take off the UAS at all. The chance of wind gusts and rapid change in the atmosphere is very high.</p>
<p><strong>Instructions for crew members</strong></p>
<p>The red cloud types (7-10) are very dangerous and almost always bring rainfall, this could be led by drizzles to heavy showers and thunderstorms with flooding. It is highly expected that the wind could blow strongly. With these cloud types, no UAS operations should be executed.</p>
<p><strong>High-Altitude Winds</strong></p>
<p>The speed of high-altitude winds is proportional to height. A force imparted on air will cause the air to move more easily when the mass of the air is less. Dense air requires a greater force to move it at the same speed as less dense air. With air density decreasing with height, it is easier to move the less dense air at a higher wind speed. Therefore, the UAS may face difficulties to overcome wind resistance when the UAS operation is carried out at a higher height. The manufacturer sets a maximum wind speed at which the UAS can still hold the position in the air firmly. Bear also in mind that the wind frequently changes in direction as height increases as well.</p>
<p>Since wind strength plays an important role in the development of turbulence, this element will be included in the safety analysis for flying in the vicinity of an object.</p>
<p>There is a mathematical formula for calculating the wind at height and various tools have been developed. One of them can be found on the website of ‘<a href="https://wind-data.ch/tools/profile.php">The Swiss Wind Power Data Website</a>’.</p>
</article>
<p></p>
<article id="article_UrcyrVjI9Bp0zGT81Wpr" class="not_editable" data-id="UrcyrVjI9Bp0zGT81Wpr">
<h2 id="mcetoc_1fmhnu757al6" class="mceNonEditable" data-id="UrcyrVjI9Bp0zGT81Wpr"><span class="shortcode">[D1.8]</span> <span class="name">Kp-index (Solar Activity)</span></h2>
<p>The Kp-index is a measurement of the Earth’s magnetic field caused by the solar wind. This type of interference is caused by things like solar activity and has a huge impact on your ability to safely operate the UAS via GNSS. Solar activity interferes with GNSS signals;</p>
<ul>
<li>It decreases the signal-to-noise ratio and affects carrier frequency, causing the receiver to lose lock on some satellites.</li>
<li>It changes the propagation delay through the ionosphere, making GNSS positioning inaccurate.</li>
</ul>
<p>So in the worst case, poor GNSS reception means that the UAS no longer knows where it is at all. And that could result in a fly-away.</p>
<p>To prevent poor GNSS reception, it is useful to always check the level of solar activity before each flight operation.&nbsp;</p>
<p>Kp-index is expressed on a scale of 1-9. It is considered that under the level of 3, the geomagnetic activity is ‘quiet’, while at 4 it is ‘disturbed’ and over 4 that we are under a magnetic storm.&nbsp;</p>
<p><strong>Instruction for crew members</strong></p>
<p>When the Kp-index is higher than 5, there is a magnetic storm. Under such a circumstance, it is strongly advised that no flight operation is executed.</p>
<p>You can download a free app (Hover - Drone&amp;UAV pilot) to your smartphone. You can also check this webpage (<a href="https://www.spaceweatherlive.com">SpaceWeatherLive.com</a>) for real-time solar activity.</p>
</article>
<p></p>
<article id="article_pmYVPtysyiZRCoF8qYwB" class="not_editable" data-id="pmYVPtysyiZRCoF8qYwB">
<h2 id="mcetoc_1fmhnu757al7" class="mceNonEditable" data-id="pmYVPtysyiZRCoF8qYwB"><span class="shortcode">[D1.9]</span> <span class="name">Disruption of GNSS signal</span></h2>
<p>The GNSS receiver in the UAS receives signals from GNSS. With this, the GNSS computer determines the position of the UAS.</p>
<p>The GNSS position accuracy is decreased due to:</p>
<ul>
<li>Changing the satellite coverage.</li>
<li>Changing in signal rate due to more/less free electrons in the ionosphere (influenced by sun activity).</li>
<li>Changing by air pressure, humidity and temperature in the troposphere.</li>
<li>Height deviation in connection with the height modulation of the earth's surface.</li>
<li>Reflection of the GNSS signal on a water surface.<span style="font-family: -apple-system, BlinkMacSystemFont, 'Segoe UI', Roboto, Oxygen, Ubuntu, Cantarell, 'Open Sans', 'Helvetica Neue', sans-serif;">&nbsp;</span></li>
</ul>
<p>In addition to the GNSS's inaccuracy of 10 meters or less, other influences cause the inaccuracy to be taken (temporarily);</p>
<ul>
<li>Signal blockage; the GNSS signal is usually weak and is quickly blocked by trees in a forest or buildings in a city area.</li>
<li>Multipath: a reflection of the signal, which makes finding the signal takes a long time or fails.</li>
<li>Disturbance: the weak GNSS signal is easy to disrupt with a stronger signal. GNSS receivers are currently protected against jamming for this.</li>
</ul>
<p><strong>Instructions for crew members</strong></p>
<p>In emergency procedures, mitigation measures have been taken to prevent the loss or weakening of the GNSS signal. Read the chapter ‘Flight Preparation’ for more information.</p>
</article>
<p></p>
<article id="article_Tj8OofpthJM57apErqAE" class="not_editable" data-id="Tj8OofpthJM57apErqAE">
<h2 class="mceNonEditable" data-id="Tj8OofpthJM57apErqAE" id="mcetoc_1in3piidfjc"><span class="shortcode">[D1.10]</span> <span class="name">Electromagnetic (EM) Fields</span></h2>
<p>Electromagnetic (EM) fields are a combination of invisible electric and magnetic fields of force. They are generated by natural phenomena, but also by human activities, mainly through the use of electricity. Mobile phones, power lines and transmitters are examples of equipment that generates EM fields.</p>
<p>The strength of EM fields is increased when you are in proximity to the source, i.e., EM filed. The strength of EM fields is decreased when you take a distance from the source. The conductor, device or transmitter that produces the EM fields is called the source. The number of waves that pass a fixed point in unit time is called, in physics, frequency. The hertz<strong>&nbsp;</strong>is the derived unit of frequency in the International System of Units (SI) and is defined as one cycle per second.&nbsp;</p>
<p>It is particularly important for conducting a UAS operation to realize that EM fields can interrupt the proper functioning of the compass in the UAS. Besides, strong electromagnetic fields can unfavourably affect working electrical parts of the UAS and cause a malfunction. It is advised that EM fields should be avoided for these reasons. Or a distance of 25 meters from the EM field should be continuously monitored to operate the UAS safely.&nbsp;&nbsp;</p>
<p>In the EM field generated by human activities, there are mainly two sources;</p>
<ul>
<li>As a by-product of the electricity generation, transport or use of electricity. Examples are high-voltage lines, electric motors and welding equipment.</li>
<li>To heat materials or send information. Examples are induction heaters and transmission installations for mobile telecommunication.</li>
</ul>
<p><strong>Examples of objects that create a strong EM field&nbsp;</strong></p>
<table width="633" style="width: 755px; border-collapse: collapse; border-color: #95a5a6; border-style: solid;" border="1">
<tbody>
<tr>
<td width="321" style="width: 395px; border-color: #95a5a6; border-style: solid;">
<p><strong>Low frequency</strong></p>
</td>
<td width="312" style="width: 331px; border-color: #95a5a6; border-style: solid;">
<p><strong>High frequency</strong>&nbsp;</p>
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td width="321" style="width: 395px; border-color: #95a5a6; border-style: solid;">
<p>Solar inverters</p>
<p>High-voltage power pylons and supply cables</p>
<p>Overhead lines for trains</p>
<p>Stadium lights (High-intensity discharge lamps)</p>
<p>Locations where electricity is generated</p>
<p>Incinerators</p>
<p>Areas with ranging fire</p>
<p>Large electric motors</p>
<p>Welding equipment</p>
</td>
<td width="312" style="width: 331px; border-color: #95a5a6; border-style: solid;">
<p style="text-align: left;">Transmission towers</p>
<p style="text-align: left;">Distribution transformers</p>
<p style="text-align: left;">Moving trains</p>
</td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table>
<p>&nbsp;The above-mentioned EM fields could disturb the GNSS signal of the UAS. As a result of that, A safe flight operation could not be executed. Possible phenomena are;</p>
<ul>
<li>No response to the smart controller</li>
<li>Unexpected flight moves</li>
<li>Fly-away due to disruption of UAS internal systems</li>
</ul>
<p><strong>Instruction for crew members</strong></p>
<p>The UA should be away from the EM field as soon as possible.</p>
</article>
<h1 id="mcetoc_1euu04s2027c"><div class="page-break"></div>Chapter D2: TRAINING<span style="font-size: 10pt; color: #ced4d9;">[TE]</span></h1>
<article id="article_Amg1RhPJCEGzwnk6E53X" class="not_editable" data-id="Amg1RhPJCEGzwnk6E53X">
<h2 class="mceNonEditable" data-id="Amg1RhPJCEGzwnk6E53X" id="mcetoc_1in3piidfjd"><span class="shortcode">[D2.1]</span> <span class="name">Introduction Training</span></h2>
<p>The ‘specific’ category may cover a wide range of UAS operations with different levels of risk and a wide range of UAS designs, in particular in terms of level of automation. The following guidelines may, therefore, have to be adapted considering the level of automation and the level of involvement of the remote pilot in the management of the flight. The UAS operator is, therefore, required to identify the competency required for the remote pilot according to the outcome of the risk&nbsp; assessment.&nbsp;</p>
<p>To be part of an operation, the [OPERATOR] flight crew must have the required basic knowledge to work with UAS and be aware of current procedures, legislation, emergency procedures and technical possibilities. Also, the flight crew must be current at all times, this means; be able to provide evidence of recent practical experience and completed training modules.&nbsp;Training is also important for working on the "just culture". Safety issues of the past period are also discussed during the training.</p>
<p>To guarantee this level of knowledge within [OPERATOR], we organize initial training courses when a flight crew member enters employment, followed by annual retraining.</p>
<p>The initial and annual training modules consist of 4 parts:</p>
<ol>
<li>Theoretical training</li>
<li>Practical training on normal operational and contingency procedures</li>
<li>Practical training for specific endorsement modules</li>
<li>Practical training emergency procedures</li>
</ol>
<p>Our pilots demonstrate that they are current by submitting a logbook. It contains the number of flight hours which are necessary to stay current, see <a href="#article_U8QokLqkpaHSPBQWuvaK">chapter A3.2 Currency Requirements.</a></p>
<p>Retraining consists of elements from the initial training, supplemented with current events and any incidents and/or cases that are relevant from the own organization.</p>
<p>All training courses are concluded by processing the results in a written evaluation and discussing the results of the training with the flight crew.</p>
</article>
<p></p>
<article id="article_0VAViySpl88KkWOwUcnz" class="not_editable" data-id="0VAViySpl88KkWOwUcnz">
<h2 class="mceNonEditable" data-id="0VAViySpl88KkWOwUcnz" id="mcetoc_1in3piidfje"><span class="shortcode">[D2.2]</span> <span class="name">Theoretical Training </span></h2>
<p>[OPERATOR] proposes to the competent authority, as part of the application, a theoretical knowledge training course for the remote pilot based on the elements that are listed in AMC1 UAS.OPEN.020(4)(b), in UAS.OPEN.040(3), in AMC1 UAS.OPEN.030(2)(c) and in Attachment A to the Annex of the UAS Regulation, which are relevant for the intended operation, complemented by the elements listed below.&nbsp;</p>
<p>Aviation safety:</p>
<ol style="list-style-type: lower-roman;">
<li>remote pilot records;</li>
<li>logbooks and associated documentation;</li>
<li>good airmanship principles;</li>
<li>aeronautical decision-making;</li>
<li>ground safety;</li>
<li>air safety;</li>
<li>air proximity reporting; and&nbsp;</li>
<li>advanced airmanship:
<ol style="list-style-type: upper-alpha;">
<li>manoeuvres and emergency procedures; and</li>
<li>general information on unusual conditions (e.g. stalls, spins, vertical lift limitations, autorotation, vortex ring states);.</li>
</ol>
</li>
</ol>
<p>Aviation regulations:</p>
<ol style="list-style-type: lower-roman;">
<li>introduction to the UAS Regulation with focus on the ‘specific’ category;</li>
<li>risk assessment, introduction to the SORA; and</li>
<li>overview of the STSs and the PDRA;.</li>
</ol>
<p>Navigation:</p>
<ol style="list-style-type: lower-roman;">
<li>navigational aids (e.g. GNSS) and their limitations</li>
<li>reading maps and aeronautical charts (e.g. 1:500 000 and 1:250 000, interpretation, specialised charts, helicopter routes, U-space service areas, and understanding of basic terms); and</li>
<li>vertical navigation (e.g. reference altitudes and heights, altimetry);.</li>
</ol>
<p>Human performance limitations:</p>
<ol style="list-style-type: lower-roman;">
<li>perception (situational awareness in BVLOS operations);</li>
<li>fatigue:
<ol style="list-style-type: upper-alpha;">
<li>flight duration within work hours;</li>
<li>circadian rhythm;</li>
<li>work stress;</li>
<li>vision problems; and</li>
<li>commercial pressure; and</li>
</ol>
</li>
<li>&nbsp;attentiveness:
<ol style="list-style-type: upper-alpha;">
<li>eliminating distractions; and</li>
<li>scan techniques;</li>
</ol>
</li>
<li>medical fitness (health precautions, alcohol, drugs, medication, ); and</li>
<li>environmental factors such as vision changes from orientation to the sun.;</li>
</ol>
<p>Airspace operating principles<span style="text-decoration: line-through;">:</span></p>
<ol style="list-style-type: lower-roman;">
<li>airspace classifications and operating principles;</li>
<li>U-space;</li>
<li>procedures for airspace reservation;</li>
<li>(iv)&nbsp; aeronautical information publications (AIPs); and</li>
<li>NOTAMs.</li>
</ol>
<ul></ul>
<p>General knowledge of UASs and external systems that support the operation of UASs:</p>
<ol style="list-style-type: lower-roman;">
<li>differences between autonomy levels (e.g. automatic versus autonomous operations);</li>
<li>loss of signal and system failure protocols — understanding the condition and planning for programmed responses such as returning to home, loiter, landing immediately;</li>
<li>equipment to mitigate air and ground risks (e.g. flight termination systems);<span style="text-decoration: line-through;"> </span></li>
<li>flight control modes;</li>
<li>the means to monitor the UA (its position, height, speed, C2 link, systems status, );</li>
<li>the means of communication with the VOs; and</li>
<li>the means to support air traffic</li>
</ol>
<p>Meteorology:</p>
<ol style="list-style-type: lower-roman;">
<li>obtaining and interpreting advanced weather information:
<ol style="list-style-type: upper-alpha;">
<li>weather reporting resources;</li>
<li>reports;</li>
<li>forecasts and meteorological conventions appropriate for typical UAS flight operations;</li>
<li>local weather assessments (including sea breeze, sea breeze front, and urban heat island);</li>
<li>low-level charts; and</li>
<li>METAR, SPECI, TAF;</li>
</ol>
</li>
<li>regional weather effects — standard weather patterns in coastal, mountain or desert terrains; and</li>
<li>weather effects on the UA (wind, storms, mist, variation of wind with altitude, wind shear,).<span style="text-decoration: line-through;">;</span></li>
</ol>
<p>Technical and operational mitigation measures for air risks:</p>
<ol style="list-style-type: lower-roman;">
<li>operations for which airspace observers (AOs) are employed; and</li>
<li>principles of detect and avoid (DAA).</li>
</ol>
<ul></ul>
<p>Operational procedures:</p>
<ol style="list-style-type: lower-roman;">
<li>mission planning, airspace considerations, and site risk assessment:
<ol style="list-style-type: upper-alpha;">
<li>measures to comply with the limitations and conditions applicable to the operational volume and to the ground risk buffer for the intended UAS operation;</li>
<li>UAS operations over a controlled ground area;</li>
<li>BVLOS operations;</li>
<li>EVLOS operations;</li>
<li>use of UA VOs;</li>
<li>importance of on-site inspections, operation planning, pre-flight and operating procedures;</li>
</ol>
</li>
<li>multi-crew cooperation (MCC):
<ol style="list-style-type: upper-alpha;">
<li>coordination between the remote pilot and other personnel (e.g. AOs) in charge of duties essential to the UAS operation;</li>
<li>crew resource management (CRM):
<ol style="list-style-type: lower-alpha;">
<li>effective leadership;</li>
<li>working with</li>
</ol>
</li>
<li>Managing data sources regarding:
<ol style="list-style-type: lower-roman;">
<li>where to obtain the data from;</li>
<li>the security of the data;</li>
<li>the quantity of the data needed; and</li>
<li>the impact on the storage of</li>
</ol>
</li>
</ol>
</li>
</ol>
<p>Emergency response plan (ERP):</p>
<p>The UAS operator provides its personnel with competency-based theoretical training covering the ERP that includes the related proficiency requirements and recurrent training.</p>
<p>Both the training and the assessment should be appropriate to the level of automation of the intended UAS operation.</p>
<p><span style="font-size: 8pt;">Source:&nbsp;AMC1 UAS.SPEC.050(1)(d) and UAS.SPEC.050(1)(e) Responsibilities of the UAS operator</span></p>
<p>Ethical Guideline Training</p>
<p>This training provides comprehensive on ethical considerations and best practices for minimizing the impact of drone operations on people and the environment. It also emphasizes the importance of responsible behavior.</p>
<ul>
<li>Respect privacy: Prioritize the privacy of individuals and property by avoiding flights over private spaces without proper consent. Ensure that any data collected during flights is handled and stored securely to protect sensitive information.</li>
<li>Minimize disruption: Reduce noise and visual disturbances by flying drones in specific zones and avoiding unnecessary flights over residential neighborhoods, parks, or wildlife habitats.</li>
<li>Engage in dialogue: Cultivate transparent communication with communities and stakeholders by proactively handling issues and offering insights into the aims and advantages of drone operations. Encourage feedback and promptly resolve any issues or misconceptions that arise.&nbsp;</li>
<li>Promote Positive Interactions: Promote constructive interactions between drone operators and the public by demonstrating professionalism, courtesy, and respect in all interactions. Utilize opportunities to educate individuals about drone technology and its various applications.</li>
<li>Stay Informed: Stay informed about developments in drone regulations, technology, and best practices through ongoing education and training. Continuously assess and update ethical guidelines to reflect evolving societal norms and expectations.</li>
</ul>
<p><strong>Currency training (internally)</strong></p>
<p>During the theoretical recurrency training, the emphasis will be on the required basic knowledge necessary to operate safely with a UA. Besides, the most up-to-date operational manual of [OPERATOR] is reviewed.&nbsp;</p>
<p>The theoretical training is also an opportunity to evaluate any incidents that have occurred in the past period.</p>
</article>
<p></p>
<article id="article_VSzniECXPnOTnHllX654" class="not_editable" data-id="VSzniECXPnOTnHllX654">
<h2 class="mceNonEditable" data-id="VSzniECXPnOTnHllX654"><span class="shortcode">[D2.3]</span> <span class="name">Practical Training on Operational Procedures</span></h2>
<p>Regarding the practical-skills training and assessment for the remote pilot, [OPERATOR] considered the competencies that are defined in AMC2 UAS.OPEN.030(2)(b), complemented by the items listed below. [OPERATOR] adapted the practical-skills training to the characteristics of the intended UAS operation and the functions available on the UAS. [OPERATOR] uses the same listed topics and may provide a practical training course also for all other personnel in charge of duties essential to the UAS operation. Appropriate simulators may be used to conduct some or all the tasks.</p>
<p>Appropriate simulators may be used to conduct some or all the tasks.</p>
<p>1. Preparation of the UAS operation:</p>
<ol style="list-style-type: lower-roman;">
<li>implement the necessary measures to comply with the limitations and conditions applicable to the operational volume and to the ground risk buffer for the intended UAS operation in accordance with the OM procedures;</li>
<li>follow the necessary procedures for UAS operations in controlled airspace, including a protocol to communicate with the ATC and obtain clearance and instructions, if necessary;</li>
<li>confirm that all necessary documents for the intended UAS operation are on-site;</li>
<li>brief all participants on the planned UAS operation;</li>
<li>perform visual airspace scanning; and</li>
<li>if AOs are employed, place them appropriately and brief them on the deconfliction scheme that includes phraseology.</li>
</ol>
<p>2. Preparation for the flight:</p>
<ol style="list-style-type: lower-roman;">
<li>ensure that all safety systems and functions, if installed on the UAS, including its height and speed limitation systems, flight termination system, and triggering system, are operational; and</li>
<li>know the basic actions to be taken in the event of an emergency, including issues with the UAS, or a mid-air collision hazard arising during the flight.</li>
<li>Perform a product inspection of the UA/equipment to ensure that the UAS is in a condition for safe operation and in accordance with the approved Concept of Operations (ConOps). (#OSO 7, criterion 2)</li>
</ol>
<p>3. Flight under abnormal conditions:</p>
<ol style="list-style-type: lower-roman;">
<li>manage a partial or a complete power shortage of the UA propulsion system, while ensuring the safety of third parties on the ground;</li>
<li>manage a situation of a non-involved person entering the operational volume or the controlled ground area, and take appropriate measures to maintain safety; and</li>
<li>react to, and take the appropriate corrective actions for, a situation where the UA is likely to exceed the limits of both the flight geography (contingency procedures) and of the operational volume (emergency procedures) as they were defined during the flight preparation.</li>
</ol>
<p>4. In general, emphasis should be placed on the following:</p>
<ol style="list-style-type: lower-roman;">
<li>normal, contingency, and emergency procedures;</li>
<li>skill tests combined with periodic proficiency checks;</li>
<li>operational experience (with on-the-job training counting towards proficiency);</li>
<li>pre-flight and post-flight procedures and documentation;</li>
<li>recurrent training (UAS / flight training device (FTD)); and</li>
<li>remote pilot incapacitation.</li>
</ol>
<p>The practical-skills training may be conducted with the UAS or on an Scenario-based training (SBT) with highly structured, real-world experience scripts for the intended UAS operation should be used to fortify personnel’s learning in an operational environment and&nbsp;improve situational awareness. SBT should include realistic normal, abnormal, and emergency&nbsp;scenarios that are drafted considering specific learning objectives.</p>
<p>The practical-skills training is checked during the assessment and can be provided using the actual UAS or an FTD appropriate to the intended UAS operation.</p>
<p><strong>Initial and recurrent training</strong></p>
<p>[OPERATOR] ensures that specified minimum requirements regarding the time of the initial and recurrent training (e.g. duration and number of flight hours) are provided for in a manner that is acceptable and approved by the competent authority.</p>
<p>Depending on the training course, each of the topics shown in Table 1 below may require only overview training or in-depth training. In-depth training should be interactive and should include discussions, case-study reviews, and role play, as deemed necessary to enhance learning. In case of change or update of the software/hardware of the UAS, depending on the size of the changes, the UAS operator should define the level of training.</p>

<article id="article_aeacphwCQXjJiVZGojHp" class="not_editable" data-id="aeacphwCQXjJiVZGojHp">
<h2 class="mceNonEditable" data-id="aeacphwCQXjJiVZGojHp"><span class="shortcode">[D2.4]</span> <span class="name">Practical Training for Contingency Operational Procedures</span></h2>
<p><span data-preserver-spaces="true">During the practical training, contingency operational procedures are discussed that deviate from the standard operating procedure at a safety level. These are specific procedures such as; unmanned aircraft leaves the flight geography or uninvolved person enters the controlled ground area. These operations require more of the flight crew than the standard operation, so it is important to train the flight crew to deal with these situations. Before each specific operation, that operation will be trained at least once during a practical simulation training to familiarize the flight crew with the additional procedures.</span></p>
<p><span data-preserver-spaces="true">After the practical training for contingency operational procedures, the flight crew member has:</span></p>
<ul>
<li><span data-preserver-spaces="true">Specific knowledge and experience in working with the deviating procedures</span></li>
<li><span data-preserver-spaces="true">Practical experience working with the UA under different circumstances</span></li>
<li><span data-preserver-spaces="true">Practical experience in executing additional safety measures</span></li>
</ul>
</article>
<p></p>
<article class="not_editable" data-id="PPKnfT1AumNWRVWBBCi4" id="article_PPKnfT1AumNWRVWBBCi4">
<h2 class="mceNonEditable" data-id="PPKnfT1AumNWRVWBBCi4" id="mcetoc_1in3piidfjh"><span class="shortcode">[D2.4A]</span> <span class="name">Training MCC (Multi Crew Coordination)</span></h2>
<p>Each [OPERATOR] crew member has followed an additional module on multi crew coordination. According to OSO #16 of SORA:</p>
<ul>
<li>An additional MCC module for a (internal or external) training must be taken and included in a customized syllabus. Administration must be carried out to keep track of who has followed the training.</li>
</ul>
<p>The program contains the following components:</p>
<ul>
<li>Use of commands;</li>
<li>Considerations about the orientation of the UAS;</li>
<li>Use of hand signals;</li>
<li>Emergency procedures.</li>
</ul>
</article>
<p></p>
<article id="article_9MmFXZjgMkLvw5Be70Gy" class="not_editable" data-id="9MmFXZjgMkLvw5Be70Gy">
<h2 class="mceNonEditable" data-id="9MmFXZjgMkLvw5Be70Gy"><span class="shortcode">[D2.5]</span> <span class="name">ERP training syllabus</span></h2>
<p>[OPERATOR] has developed a training syllabus that contains all elements of the ERP forAMC3 UAS. SPEC.030(3)(e) ED2022/002/R Emergency Response Plan (ERP) with 'medium' and 'high' robustness level. &nbsp;This section describes the necessary skills and areas of knowledge needed to meet:</p>
<table width="100%">
<tbody>
<tr>
<td width="17%">
<p><strong>Area</strong></p>
</td>
<td width="81%">
<p><strong>Learning objectives</strong></p>
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td width="17%">
<p>Generic</p>
</td>
<td width="81%">
<p>Knowledge of the situation, scope and requirements for an ERP; The student can describe the principles of ERP. <em>Background: from the regulation is in "AMC3 UAS. SPEC.030(3)(e)" includes the definition of the content of an ERP and the methodology for its validation. It can be used to meet criterion #1 (Procedures) of Mitigation M3 – An ERP is present, UAS operator validated and effective from Appendix B to AMC1 Article 11 ( SORA) for medium and high robustness; </em></p>
<p></p>
<p>Knowledge of the purpose and principles of an ERP. <em>Background: The UAS operator, in collaboration with other stakeholders, must develop, coordinate and maintain an ERP that ensures an orderly and safe transition from normal operation to emergency and return to normal operation. The ERP should include the actions to be taken by the UAS operator or specified persons in an emergency situation, and indicate the extent, nature and complexity of the activities to be performed by the UAS operator. An ERP has been specifically developed to: </em></p>
<ul>
<li style="list-style-type: none;">
<ul>
<li><em>mitigate any escalating consequences of the emergency; </em></li>
<li><em>comply with the conditions to alert the relevant authorities and entities.</em></li>
</ul>
</li>
</ul>
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td width="17%">
<p>Effective ERP</p>
</td>
<td width="81%">
<p>Knowledge of the components of a good ERP. &nbsp;<em>Background:</em> the <em>ERP must be appropriate to the nature and complexity of UA operations. E</em> and <em>ERP should contain all necessary information on the role of the relevant personnel in an emergency situation and their response to it.</em> <em>&nbsp;The ERP must be accessible to staff and include procedures and checklists for various emergencies. The ERP contains contact details of those involved. </em></p>
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td width="17%">
<p>Emergencies, response activation, procedures and checklists</p>
</td>
<td width="81%">
<p>The student knows the emergency situations and checklists of [OPERATOR]'s ERP. &nbsp;<em>Background</em>: <em>The ERP must include at least procedures for:</em></p>
<ul>
<li><em>an orderly transition from to the emergency response phase.</em></li>
<li><em>the assignment of responsibilities and roles in emergency situations;</em></li>
<li><em>coordinated action and interaction with other entities to respond to the emergency; and to return to normal work as soon as possible.</em></li>
</ul>
<p><em>[OPERATOR] has mapped out its possible emergencies in [C6.3] Activation of ERP. The corresponding checklists in [APPENDIX C6] ERP checklists are also known. Furthermore, the student is familiar with the transition process [C6.6] ERP procedure, prioritization and division of roles.</em></p>
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td width="17%">
<p>ERP organization</p>
</td>
<td width="81%">
<p>The student knows the roles and responsibilities and rolls within the ERP of [OPERATOR]. <em>&nbsp;Background: UAS operator must appoint an emergency response manager (ERM) who has full responsibility for the emergency aid and its organization.</em> <em>&nbsp;As the organization grows, then an ERT (Emergency Response Team) should be established with this task.</em></p>
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td width="17%">
<p>ERP tools</p>
</td>
<td width="81%">
<p>The student can describe the necessary tools available. &nbsp;<em>Background: the student must be familiar with the extinguishers used by [OPERATOR], personal protective </em>&nbsp;equipment, <em>visibility</em> clothing, <em>helmets, glasses, gloves</em>, <em>first aid kit, communication equipment, e.g. telephones (fixed and mobile), walkie-talkies, aviation radios, internet;</em></p>
</td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table>
<p>The training for the ERP includes a theoretical part and a practical part:</p>
<p><strong>Theory</strong></p>
<p>The candidate must demonstrate the level of knowledge about [OPERATOR]'s ERP on the following topics:</p>
<ol>
<li>Legislation, scope and requirements for an ERP</li>
<li>Purpose of the ERP</li>
<li>Effectiveness of the ERP</li>
<li>Emergency situations, activation, procedures and checklists at [OPERATOR]</li>
<li>Responsibilities of roles and key contacts at [OPERATOR]</li>
<li>ERP tools for emergency prevention</li>
<li>Emergency Responce means</li>
</ol>
<p><strong>Practical test</strong></p>
<p>In practice, emergencies (such as a crash) cannot be easily simulated. The student performs a Table-Top exercise focused on [OPERATOR]'s operations. A Table-Top exercise is an interactive form of emergency practice. An emergency is played out on paper. The basis for this is the Operational Plan of [OPERATOR].</p>
<p><strong>Frequency</strong></p>
<p>The ERP is tested annually through practical exercises involving the relevant personnel.</p>
<p>The ERP is periodically reviewed and updated by the accountable manager or the designated person responsible for the organization to maintain its effectiveness.</p>
<p><strong>Administration</strong></p>
<p>[OPERATOR] keeps a record of the ERP training completed by the crewmembers. [OPERATOR] is able to show this logbook when they are asked by the CAA.</p>
</article>
<p></p>
<article id="article_UVSP7ZfC6dMWXa4JfPFo" class="not_editable" data-id="UVSP7ZfC6dMWXa4JfPFo">
<h2 class="mceNonEditable" data-id="UVSP7ZfC6dMWXa4JfPFo" id="mcetoc_1itg4ja7124l"><span class="shortcode">[D2.6]</span> <span class="name">Training syllabus Night-VLOS (outside UDP flight)</span></h2>
<p>This section describes the skills and knowledge required to fly an UA outside of UDP according to Annex II to ED Decision 2022/002/R (Operation Specific Endorsement Module).&nbsp;</p>
<table style="border-collapse: collapse; width: 100%; height: 1470.51px; border-width: 1px; border-style: solid;" border="1">
<tbody>
<tr style="height: 34.5455px;">
<td width="171" style="width: 17.5791%; height: 34.5455px;">
<p><strong>Areas to be covered</strong></p>
</td>
<td width="296" style="width: 82.4575%; height: 34.5455px;">
<p><strong>Learning objectives</strong></p>
</td>
</tr>
<tr style="height: 837.898px;">
<td width="171" style="width: 17.5791%; height: 837.898px;">
<p>General</p>
</td>
<td width="296" style="width: 82.4575%; height: 837.898px;">
<ul>
<li>Recognise the meaning of the definition of ‘night’ or&nbsp;other similar wording that is used for night flight.&nbsp;<em>Rationale: In Regulation (EU) No 1178/2011 (the ‘Aircrew Regulation’), ‘night’ for manned aviation ‘means the period between the end of evening civil twilight and the beginning of morning civil twilight or such other period between sunset and sunrise as may be prescribed by the appropriate authority’. </em><em>Some national laws use the sunset and sunrise times for the definition of a night flight. ‘Sunset’ is defined as the daily disappearance of the upper limb of the sun below the horizon. This time depends on the&nbsp;</em><em>latitude and longitude of the viewpoint. There are </em><em>many websites and apps to find out the sunset and</em><em> sunrise times at a specific location.</em></li>
<li>Recognise the benefits of illuminating the operational area, especially during the critical phases of take-off and landing.</li>
<li>Recognise that during night flight it is hard to estimate the distance between the UA and other obstacles if visibility is only ensured by the lights of the UA.</li>
<li>Recognise that a visual obstacle avoidance system may be less accurate in night-time operations.</li>
<li>Understand that if the sight of the UA is lost at night, return-to-home (RTH) should be immediately followed. <em>Rationale: During daytime, it is sometimes difficult to see the position of the UA, which is even more difficult at night.</em></li>
<li>Recognise that an infrared radiation (IR) camera allows one to see enough at night. Turning off the front green flashing lights might improve the view because there will be no reflection in the on-board camera.</li>
<li>Recognise that the IR camera does not help in case of rain/humidity, and that the IR visibility significantly decreases.</li>
<li>Explain the use of the green flashing light at night.</li>
<li>Explain the use of navigation lights, position lights, anti-collision lights, and other lights for UA controllability.</li>
<li>Explain the use of lights (e.g. navigation, position, or anti-collision lights) for recognising the presence of manned aircraft. <em>Rationale: Those lights show the UA is positioned</em><em> and the direction in which the UA is aligned.&nbsp;</em></li>
</ul>
<p><em>For manned aircraft, a red navigation light is located</em><em> on the leading edge of the left-wing tip and a green navigation light on the leading edge of the right- wing tip (for helicopters, on the left and right sides of the cockpit). A white navigation light is positioned on the tail as far aft as possible. High-intensity strobe lights are also located in those positions. They are used as anti-collision lights and flash twice after a short break. A red rotating beacon is also part of the anti-collision lights.</em></p>
</td>
</tr>
<tr style="height: 143.92px;">
<td width="171" style="width: 17.5791%; height: 143.92px;">
<p>Degradation of visual acuity</p>
</td>
<td width="296" style="width: 82.4575%; height: 143.92px;">
<ul>
<li>Recognise that flying the UA at night degrades visual perception.&nbsp;</li>
<li>Recognise night myopia, caused by the increasing pupil size. At low-light levels, without distant objects to focus on, the focusing mechanism of the eye may go to a resting myopic position.</li>
<li>If night-vision goggles are used, know how they function.</li>
</ul>
</td>
</tr>
<tr style="height: 121.534px;">
<td width="171" style="width: 17.5791%; height: 121.534px;">
<p>Night illusions</p>
</td>
<td width="296" style="width: 82.4575%; height: 121.534px;">
<ul>
<li>Define the term ‘night illusion’.</li>
<li>Recognise and overcome visual illusions that are caused by darkness, and understand the physiological conditions that may degrade night vision.</li>
<li>State the limitations of night vision techniques at night and by day.</li>
</ul>
</td>
</tr>
<tr style="height: 211.08px;">
<td width="171" style="width: 17.5791%; height: 211.08px;">
<p>Altered&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; visual-scanning techniques</p>
</td>
<td width="296" style="width: 82.4575%; height: 211.08px;">
<ul>
<li>State the limitations of the different visual-scanning techniques at night and by day.</li>
</ul>
<p><em>Rationale: Despite the value of electronic means of</em><em> conflict detection, physical lookout remains an important defence against the loss of visual separation for all types of aircraft.</em></p>
<p><em>To avoid collisions, the remote pilot should visually scan effectively from the moment the UA starts moving until it comes to a stop at the end of the flight. Collision threats are present everywhere.</em></p>
<p><em>Before take-off, the remote pilot should visually</em><em> check the take-off area to ensure that there are no other objects. After take-off, the remote pilot should continue to visually scan to ensure a safe departure of the UA with no obstacles.</em></p>
</td>
</tr>
<tr style="height: 121.534px;">
<td width="171" style="width: 17.5791%; height: 121.534px;">
<p>Altered&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; identification of obstacles</p>
</td>
<td width="296" style="width: 82.4575%; height: 121.534px;">
<ul>
<li>Explain the effect of obstacles on the take-off distance that is required at night.</li>
</ul>
<p><em>Rationale: The remote pilot should know the flight</em><em> area where the UA will fly at night. Objects look different and power lines are nearly invisible at night. It is, therefore, advisable that the remote pilot conduct a test flight during the daytime.</em></p>
</td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table>
<p>The training for night-VLOS flights includes a theoretical test and a practical test:</p>
<p><strong>1. </strong><strong>Theoretical test</strong></p>
<p>The candidate must demonstrate his/her level of knowledge about the privileges and limitations of the N-VLOS endorsement and about the following topics:</p>
<ul>
<li>UA requirements for night flights.</li>
<li>Additional considerations for UA night flights (compared to daytime flights).</li>
<li>Rules and considerations for night flights.</li>
<li>Knows the definition of “night” within the Netherlands.</li>
<li>Understands the visual impairments as they can occur at night and recognizes the limitations in human performance that can play a role in an N-VLOS night flight, such as altered identification of obstacles and night illusions.&nbsp;</li>
<li>Knowledge of the procedure in the Operations Manual in Chapter C3.9 Outside UDP Operations.</li>
</ul>
<p><strong></strong><strong>2. Practical test</strong></p>
<p>Train on various payloads and UA configurations, operations both in dark conditions and under artificial lighting and various weather conditions (wind, cold):</p>
<ul>
<li>Ensures that the UA is ready to fly and adequately equipped for night operations.</li>
<li>Competently performs all normal maneuvers at night in Atti mode or an automated GPS mode.</li>
<li>Capable of safely and efficiently orienting and navigating the UA remote from the ground station under manual (atti) operation or in an automated mode.</li>
<li>Maintains an effective observation for other air traffic and takes adequate action to ensure adequate separation from other air traffic.&nbsp;</li>
</ul>
<p><strong>Note:</strong> Flight test elements for N-VLOS authorization can be combined in a single test or spread over several flights.<strong>&nbsp;</strong></p>
<p><span style="font-size: 8pt;">Source: Annex II to ED Decision 2022/002/R (Operation Specific Endorsement Module)</span></p>
</article>
<p></p>
<p></p>

<p class="ListLevel1"><strong><span class="Bold">6.&nbsp;Emergency procedures</span></strong></p>
</td>
<td style="width: 36.6581%; height: 22.3906px;">&nbsp;</td>

<tr style="height: 44.7812px;">
<td style="width: 59.6961%; height: 44.7812px;">
<p class="ListLevel1"><span class="Bold"><span class="Italic">(The UAS operator should define procedures to cope with emergency situations.)</span></span></p>
</td>
<td style="width: 36.6581%; height: 44.7812px;">Chapter C5: EMERGENCY PROCEDURES</td>
</tr>
<tr style="height: 44.7812px;">
<td style="width: 59.6961%; height: 44.7812px;">
<p class="ListLevel1"><strong><span class="Bold">7.&nbsp;Emergency response plan (ERP) (optional)</span></strong></p>
</td>

<article id="article_HZqMod6IwVtQbkSvAi0H" class="not_editable" data-id="HZqMod6IwVtQbkSvAi0H">
<h2 class="mceNonEditable" data-id="HZqMod6IwVtQbkSvAi0H" id="mcetoc_1h4is3uln12d"><span class="shortcode">[APPENDIX C]</span> <span class="name">Flight Plan and Check Lists</span></h2>
<p>This appendix contains the documents required for flight preparation and the necessary checklists.</p>
</article>
<article id="article_3segXFaJVQvTvnUA4feb" class="not_editable" data-id="3segXFaJVQvTvnUA4feb">
<h2 id="mcetoc_1gf5tglhe8d" class="mceNonEditable" data-id="3segXFaJVQvTvnUA4feb"><span class="shortcode">[APPENDIX C.0]</span> <span class="name">Introduction Check Lists</span></h2>
<p>Working with checklists is quite a common practice in aviation. Mandatory and consistent use of the checklists reduces the risk of errors and makes an essential contribution to flight safety.&nbsp;</p>
<p><strong>Checklist Methodologies</strong></p>
<table style="border-collapse: collapse; width: 99.6222%;" border="1">
<tbody>
<tr>
<td style="width: 21.5448%;">Name</td>
<td style="width: 74.8444%;">Description</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td style="width: 21.5448%;">Challenge &amp; Respond</td>
<td style="width: 74.8444%;">The observer reads the item to be checked. The Pilot controls the item to be checked for the exact status. The Pilot responds with the status. The observer places a checkmark on the checklist.</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td style="width: 21.5448%;">Read &amp; Do</td>
<td style="width: 74.8444%;">A crew member reads out the item to be checked. Another crew member takes the action, after which the crew member who has read the item places a checkmark on the checklist.</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td style="width: 21.5448%;">Flow</td>
<td style="width: 74.8444%;">A checklist is designed for independent use so that the user does not have to reference a manual.</td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table>
<p><strong><span data-preserver-spaces="true">Conducting Checklists</span></strong></p>
<p><span data-preserver-spaces="true">The following general rules for conducting checklists are used to support communication and understanding between the crew.&nbsp;</span></p>
<ul>
<li><span data-preserver-spaces="true">The crew conducts the checklist at a fixed schedule when the workload is low. As a result, the crew is not distracted or rushed through the checklist.&nbsp;</span></li>
<li><span data-preserver-spaces="true">The Pilot is not distracted his attention from controlling the UAS by performing tasks assigned for the observer or payload operator.</span></li>
<li><span data-preserver-spaces="true">The "responding" crew member responds to the "challenge" when the configuration is checked or corrected if necessary.</span></li>
<li><span data-preserver-spaces="true">If the requested configuration is not possible, the "responding" crew member registers the current configuration.</span></li>
<li><span data-preserver-spaces="true">The "challenging" crew member waits until he receives a confirmation. After the confirmation, he places a checkmark on the checklist. After setting a checkmark, the next checklist item should be controlled. Do not call several challenge items together while the other member replies with a series of chunked responses.&nbsp;</span></li>
<li><span data-preserver-spaces="true">When conducting the checklists is interrupted, the crew should be able to show clearly which items are already completed, which are not yet controlled.</span></li>
</ul>
<p><span data-preserver-spaces="true">Before conducting the checklists, make sure that the correct checklists are chosen.</span></p>
</article>

<tr style="height: 36px;">
<td style="text-align: center;">1. Overview map with location</td>

<tr style="height: 324px;">
<td><strong><img style="display: block; margin-left: auto; margin-right: auto; height: 310px;" src="https://firebasestorage.googleapis.com/v0/b/drone-consultancy-test.appspot.com/o/images_new%2F82HBR8nAdGoyZMspNn99%2FOperational%20Plan%20Example.jpg?alt=media&amp;token=d02267d6-5268-4cf0-b3e4-98f7a27e356d" alt="" width="389"></strong></td>

<p><strong>&nbsp;</strong></p>
<p class="p1" style="text-align: center;"></p>
<p class="p1" style="text-align: center;">2. Air Risk/Airspace Operational Volume (Godrone of LVNL)</p>
<p class="p1" style="text-align: center;"><img style="height: 197px;" src="https://firebasestorage.googleapis.com/v0/b/drone-consultancy-test.appspot.com/o/images_new%2F82HBR8nAdGoyZMspNn99%2FOperational%20Volume%20example.jpg?alt=media&amp;token=83bb5522-6cad-42d6-afe4-11102b755972" alt="" width="374"></p>

<p><span style="font-size: 10pt;">Damage &lt; 50K€</span></p>
</td>
<td width="15%" style="border-color: #ecf0f1;">
<p><span style="font-size: 10pt;">Limited impact</span></p>
</td>
<td width="11%" style="border-color: #ecf0f1;">
<p><span style="font-size: 10pt;"><strong>2</strong></span></p>
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td width="14%" style="border-color: #ecf0f1;">
<p><span style="font-size: 10pt;"><strong>NEGLIGIBLE</strong></span></p>
</td>
<td width="15%" style="border-color: #ecf0f1;">
<p><span style="font-size: 10pt;">Superficial or no injuries</span></p>
</td>
<td width="19%" style="border-color: #ecf0f1;">
<p><span style="font-size: 10pt;">Negligible or no effects</span></p>
</td>
<td width="24%" style="border-color: #ecf0f1;">
<p><span style="font-size: 10pt;">Financial loss with negligible impact&nbsp;Damage &lt; 10K€ (*)</span></p>
</td>
<td width="15%" style="border-color: #ecf0f1;">
<p><span style="font-size: 10pt;">Light or no impact</span></p>
</td>
<td width="11%" style="border-color: #ecf0f1;">
<p><span style="font-size: 10pt;"><strong>1</strong></span></p>
</td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table>
<p><span style="font-size: 10pt;"><em>*&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; </em>Indicative: depends on the size of the Company and volume of business.</span></p>
<p><span style="font-size: 10pt;"><img src="https://firebasestorage.googleapis.com/v0/b/drone-consultancy-test.appspot.com/o/images_new%2F82HBR8nAdGoyZMspNn99%2FRisk%20Assessment%20overview.png?alt=media&amp;token=34aa4dda-592c-4910-b28d-3a766edf3af2" alt=""></span></p>
<p><span style="font-size: 10pt;">Source: EHEST-Safety Management Toolkit-Guidance-NCO - V.2 30 Sep</span></p>
<ul>
<li><span style="font-size: 10pt;"><strong>Unacceptable Risk Level</strong> - the red zone in the matrix: risk is too high to continue operating.</span></li>
<li><span style="font-size: 10pt;">Action required: Prohibit/suspend the operation.&nbsp;</span></li>
<li><span style="font-size: 10pt;"><strong>Tolerable Risk Level</strong> - the yellow zone in the matrix: the risk level can be tolerated for the operation, providing that appropriate mitigation measures are in place.</span></li>
<li><span style="font-size: 10pt;">Action required: Introduce appropriate mitigation measures.</span></li>
<li><strong style="font-size: 10pt;">Acceptable Risk Level</strong><span style="font-size: 10pt;"> - the green zone in the matrix: risk is tolerable and can be accepted for the operation.</span></li>
</ul>
<p><strong>H. Emergency Respons Plan</strong></p>
<p>Activation of Emergency Respons Plan when UAS exits the Contingency Area, fire or collision with manned aircraft, person or animal.</p>

<article id="article_a3PvAvPZ8SP9In1FlDkM" class="not_editable" data-id="a3PvAvPZ8SP9In1FlDkM">
<h2 class="mceNonEditable" data-id="a3PvAvPZ8SP9In1FlDkM" id="mcetoc_1in3piidfjm"><span class="shortcode">[APPENDIX C5]</span> <span class="name">Proof of the adequacy of the contingency and emergency procedures of [OPERATOR]</span></h2>
<p>In case of [OPERATOR]s Risk Assessesment, [OPERATOR] needs to “ensure the adequacy of the contingency and emergency procedures and prove it through any of the following:</p>
<p>(a) dedicated flight tests; or</p>
<p>(b) simulations, provided that the representativeness of the simulation means is proven for the intended purpose with positive results; or</p>
<p>(c) any other means acceptable to the competent authority;”</p>
<p>[OPERATOR] proves this by:</p>
</article>
<p>See external document Format Assurance contingency and emergency procedures</p>
<p></p>
<p></p><div class="page-break"></div><p></p>
<p></p>

<h2 class="mceNonEditable" data-id="OxDazeaO5d7TkBkY6G7y"><span class="shortcode">[APPENDIX C5.2]</span> <span class="name">Safety and fitness statement form drone operations [OPERATOR]</span></h2>
<p>According to EU regulations, [OPERATOR] must ensure that all persons present in the controlled territory comply with PDRA-S01, paragraph 4, UAS operator and UAS operational conditions. This form should also be completed before the start of the operation.</p>

<h2 class="mceNonEditable" data-id="smVjIJdoPvBKlVsm2B2b" id="mcetoc_1itg4ja7224p"><span class="shortcode">[APPENDIX C6.2]</span> <span class="name">Mid-air Collision</span></h2>
<table width="100%" height="467" style="border-collapse: collapse; width: 100%; height: 209.062px; background-color: #ffffff; border-color: #95a5a6; border-style: solid; margin-left: auto; margin-right: auto;" border="1">
<tbody>
<tr style="height: 26.1375px;">
<td width="497" style="width: 74.5938%; background-color: #e03e2d; height: 26.1375px; border-color: #95a5a6;">

</td>

<td style="width: 74.5938%; height: 26.1375px; border-color: #95a5a6;">

</td>
<td style="width: 25.2924%; height: 26.1375px; border-color: #95a5a6;">
<p><span style="font-size: 12pt;">Observer</span></p>
